In re Executive Life Ins. Co.


No. B078462.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


32 Cal. App. 4th 344; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 453; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 129; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1166; 95 Daily Journal DAR 2051



February 15, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing March 15, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 241. Review Denied May 11, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3334.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS006912, Kurt J. Lewin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed in two respects. First, as to the approval of the settlement with the post-1988 Muni-GICs including any attorney fee provision related to that settlement, and second, as to the denial of interest to appellant Commercial National on interim payments it did not receive after our decision in Commercial National Bank became final. The cause is remanded for further proceedings on those issues consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant banks sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which approved the modified rehabilitation plan submitted by respondent insurance commissioner in its insurer insolvency conservatorship of a defaulted insurance company. Respondent insurer, as successor in interest to the defaulted insurance company's policies, was also named as an interested party in the proceedings.


OVERVIEW: An insurance company issued guaranteed investment contracts (GIC's), high-yield fixed income securities with no or very low credit ratings. The market for the securities bottomed and the bonds were in default. The company's reserves covering the bonds were depleted. Respondent insurance commissioner placed the insurance company in conservatorship and seized its assets. Respondent insurance commissioner crafted a rehabilitation plan in which respondent insurer issued new policies to the former policyholders of the defaulted company. Policyholders could opt out and receive the liquidation value of their claims. After one appeal, a modified rehabilitation plan was drawn. The lower court approved it. Appellant banks challenged the plan once more, arguing terms of the bond sale were unreasonable and the sales price inadequate. The court found no error in the transfer of the insurance company's business to respondent insurer or in the sale of its bonds. The court affirmed the judgment in all respects, save two reversals on minor points governing the priority of post-1988 municipalities' GIC's, along with its associated attorney's fees, and one appellant bank's interim payments.


OUTCOME: The court generally affirmed the lower court's judgment approving of the modified rehabilitation plan, but reversed in two respects. It reversed the settlement's approval of the post-1988 municipalities' guaranteed investment contracts, including related attorney's fee provisions. Second, the denial of interest to one appellant bank for interim payments until the appellate decision became final was reversed. The cause was remanded.

 

828 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Gates v. Superior Court


No. B083893.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


32 Cal. App. 4th 481; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 489; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 130; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1146; 95 Daily Journal DAR 2021



February 15, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied May 18, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3393.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. BC079932, BC079933, and BC079934, John H. Leahy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is granted. The respondent court is directed to set aside its order of March 14, 1994, overruling the demurrers as to the fifth and sixth causes of action and to enter a new order sustaining the demurrers to the fifth and sixth causes of action of all three first amended complaints without leave to amend. All parties are to bear their own costs in connection with these extraordinary writ proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner senior police commanders sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to sustain a demurrer on two of six causes of action in the first amended complaints brought by plaintiffs in respondent court. Petitioners contended that under plaintiffs' two state law causes of action no monetary damages could be recovered for the alleged discriminatory deployment of police officers during a riot.


OVERVIEW: Following the acquittal of police officers who were tried for excessive use of force, a riot broke out. The plaintiffs were attacked by a mob. They alleged in the trial court that the police department's policy of discrimination, and distribution of police resources, denied them of sufficient police protection services during the riot, thereby causing them personal injuries. The police were withdrawn from minority communities pursuant to petitioner senior police commanders' orders and deployed to predominantly Anglo populations. Plaintiffs alleged civil rights violations under Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51.7, 52. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate that respondent trial court sustain their demurrer to the two civil rights causes of action brought by the plaintiffs. The court issued a writ of mandate, finding that the demurrers should have been sustained, without leave to amend, as to the plaintiffs' two state law claims. The Torts Claims Act provided an immunity from money damages for the failure to provide sufficient police protective services during a riot. That racially neutral immunity did not violate the Equal Protection Clauses of the United States or California Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate, holding that, as to plaintiffs' two state law civil rights claims, petitioner senior police commanders' demurrers should have been sustained without leave to amend. It directed respondent court to enter a new order sustaining the demurrers on the first amended complaints without leave to amend. Petitioners were immune from money damages and the racially neutral immunity was not unconstitutional.

 

829 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ortiz


No. C016841.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


32 Cal. App. 4th 286; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 59; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 120; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1098; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1923



February 10, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. *


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied May 10, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3244.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. SC055545A, Stephen G. Demetras, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), convicting him of possession of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of heroin and filed a motion to suppress the evidence seized by police in a warrantless search of his hotel room. The trial court denied defendant's motion and defendant was convicted of possession of heroin. Defendant appealed, contending that there were no exigent circumstances to justify the warrantless search. The court affirmed, holding that there were exigent circumstances justifying the search because, at the time of the arrest and seizure, the facts known to the officer and the inferences drawn therefrom were sufficient to lead a reasonable officer to conclude that there was an imminent danger that the contraband he had seen in plain view would be destroyed if he did not act immediately to arrest defendant and seize the evidence. Therefore, the warrantless entry into the hotel room to arrest defendant and seize the contraband was legal, and the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence, holding that there were exigent circumstances to justify the warrantless search. Therefore, the arrest and seizure of the contraband were proper.

 

830 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Culpepper


No. A066016.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


32 Cal. App. 4th 237; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 203; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 114; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1069; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1880



February 9, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied May 18, 1995 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports, (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3384.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 120312, Brenda Harbin-Forte, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Oakland-Piedmont-Emeryville Judicial District of Alameda County sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted appellant of grand theft from the person. On the basis of one prior felony conviction, the trial court sentenced appellant to state prison for five years pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(c).


OVERVIEW: Appellant complained that his sentence under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(c)(5), violated his right to equal protection because it authorized fewer total conduct credits against his sentence for theft than a person convicted of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190 could earn. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, noting that appellant's contention was based on case authority that the number of credits murderers could earn was dictated by an initiative measure, and thus could not be modified by a later legislative enactment such as Section 667(c)(5). However, the court pointed out that the exact language of Section 667(c)(5), which reduced the total credits that could be earned to one-fifth of one's sentence, was enacted by voter initiative as Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12(a)(5). By the very reasoning cited by appellant, Section 667(c)(5) effectively changed the total number of credits that could be earned by murderers under the scheme of Section 190 from one-third to one-fifth of the sentence. Therefore, Section 667(c)(5) did not adopt a classification that affected persons convicted of murder differently than persons convicted of other crimes.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction and sentence were affirmed. The statute at issue did not provide for fewer total conduct credits against sentences for thefts and other crimes than another statute provided for murder. Rather, the statute provided a limit to the number of credits that murderers could earn under the other statutory scheme. Therefore, the statute presented no equal protection issue.

 

831 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Rains v. Belshe


No. A063119.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


32 Cal. App. 4th 157; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 185; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 107; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1044; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1813



February 8, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 949165, William J. Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter a new order denying the petition. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which concluded that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1418.8, in its preamended form, was unconstitutional as violating the privacy and due process rights of nursing home patients who lacked capacity to give informed consent to recommended medical intervention.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1418.8 was enacted to allow certain incompetent patients residing in skilled nursing facilities or intermediate care facilities to receive medical treatment, after a physician had determined a patient's incapacity to give informed consent to such treatment and an interdisciplinary review team had determined the treatment was medically appropriate. Respondent filed a petition for a writ of mandate, seeking to invalidate the statute, as originally enacted, on constitutional grounds and stay its enforcement. The lower court concluded that the section, in its preamended form, was unconstitutional in that it violated the constitutional privacy rights and due process rights of nursing home patients who lacked capacity to give informed consent to recommended medical intervention. The court, reviewing the amended version of the statute, held that the statute was constitutional. It reversed the lower court and remanded the cause to the trial court with directions to enter an order denying the petition. The court found that the statute did not violate the constitutional privacy rights or due process rights of those nursing home patients.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause to the trial court. The statute was constitutional. It did not violate the constitutional privacy rights or due process rights of those nursing home patients who are determined by a physician to lack capacity to give informed consent to recommended medical intervention, and who did not have another person with legal authority to give that consent.

 

832 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Mundy v. Superior Court


No. G015679.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


31 Cal. App. 4th 1396; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 568; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 70; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 823; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1448



January 31, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 27, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 714274, Dennis Keough, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: In 1994, the Legislature passed a new forfeiture law but decided the law in effect on December 31, 1993, shall govern forfeiture proceedings commenced before January 1, 1994. While this determination deprives Mundy of some of the procedural advantages which accrued under the forfeiture statutes after January 1, 1994, those benefits are primarily prospective in their application to this case. Accordingly, the new law does not violate ex post facto principles. Nor does it violate Mundy's equal protection rights, given the Legislature's prerogative to make statutory amendments prospective only. Therefore, the district attorney may continue to pursue the forfeiture claim against Mundy under the statutes in effect on December 31, 1993. The alternative writ is discharged, and the petition for writ of prohibition/mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) dismissing his complaint that challenged the constitutionality of the newly enacted asset forfeiture law as set forth in Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11469.


OVERVIEW: Police seized 1,049 grams of marijuana and $ 24,796 from petitioner's home during a warrant search. The people filed a complaint to have the money forfeited as narcotics related. The trial court determined that while the 1993 forfeiture law had expired, the forfeiture provisions as they existed in 1988 remained effective. Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of the newly enacted asset forfeiture law as set forth in Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11469. The court affirmed because the law withstood constitutional scrutiny. Although petitioner was deprived of some procedural advantages, those benefits were primarily prospective in their application. Accordingly, the law did not violate ex post facto principles. In addition, the law did not violate petitioner's equal protection rights because the legislature had the prerogative to make statutory amendments prospective only.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing petitioner's challenge to the constitutionality of the asset forfeiture law was affirmed because the law did not violate ex post facto principles or petitioner's equal protection rights. The legislature had the prerogative to make statutory amendments prospective only.

Harris Transportation Co. v. Air Resources Board


No. C016988.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


32 Cal. App. 4th 1472; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 431; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 213



January 31, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 2, 1995. The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court for Unpublished to Published March 2, 1995. Review Denied April 19, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2697.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV374301, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff drivers challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandate challenging enforcement of the Heavy-Duty Vehicle Inspection Program, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 13, § 2180- 2187, adopted by defendant California Air Resources Board.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court denied plaintiff drivers' petition for writ of mandate, plaintiff appealed alleging that the Heavy-Duty Vehicle Inspection Program (HDVIP), Cal. Code Regs. tit. 13, § 2180- 2187, adopted by defendant California Air Resources Board (Board) was unlawful. The court affirmed the denial of the writ. Because plaintiffs failed to raise the issue of whether the tests were properly conducted in the administrative proceeding, they waived that argument and therefore were not entitled to relief under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. In addition, instead of challenging the regulations themselves, plaintiffs contended that the regulations were not being properly administered and that they were therefore entitled to a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1085. However, in any future proceedings to enforce HDVIP, the cited party could challenge a citation on the basis of lack of correlation of the test equipment. Therefore, plaintiffs had an adequate remedy in the ordinary course of law and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the writ.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that plaintiff drivers' petition for writ of mandate, which challenged defendant California Air Resources Board's Heavy-Duty Vehicle Inspection Program, should be denied because plaintiffs failed to exhaust their administrative remedies and they had an adequate remedy at law.

Baluyut v. Superior Court


No. H012714.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


40 Cal. App. 4th 1736; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 741; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 66; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 790; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1390



January 30, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 11, 1995 (S045419), REPORTED AT: 1995 CAL. LEXIS 3776.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface 2o Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 31 Cal. App. 4th 1334 and 36 Cal. App. 4th 38.)] (See 12 Cal. 4th 826)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. CV738881, Thomas C. Edwards, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the relief prayed for is granted and the municipal court is directed to grant petitioners' motion to dismiss.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied their petition for a writ of mandate to vacate its earlier denial of petitioners' motion to dismiss the charges against them for disorderly conduct under Cal. Penal Code § 647(a), because it found that petitioners had not been denied equal protection of the law by discriminatory enforcement.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court denied petitioners' writ of mandamus, they appealed. The court reversed and held that the writ to vacate a prior order denying petitioners' motion to dismiss should have been granted. The trial court erred in holding that petitioners were required to prove a prosecutor's specific intent to punish a defendant for membership in a group. Although this may be one method of proving discriminatory enforcement, it is not the only method. Petitioners proved that they would not have been arrested except for the invidious discrimination against them and that homophobia accounted for the course of conduct by the police department. Moreover, no rational reason for the deliberate structuring of the decoy operation to focus on homosexuals was shown, leading to the conclusion that no reason for it existed other than hostility toward homosexuals. Such a showing was sufficient to establish an equal protection violation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and granted petitioners' writ of mandamus, holding that their motion to dismiss should have been granted because they proved that their equal protection rights were violated by the police's selective enforcement of the statute prohibiting disorderly conduct.

Stubblefield Construction Co. v. City of San Bernardino


Nos. E009749, E010088.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


32 Cal. App. 4th 687; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 413; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 155



January 27, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 23, 1995. The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published February 23, 1995 on Denial of Rehearing, Reported at: 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 156. Review Denied April 20, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2745. Counsel Corrected September 16, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, Nos. 242998 and 252058, Don A. Turner, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the San Bernardino Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The order dated June 10, 1991, denying the City's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict is reversed. The order granting interim attorney fees to plaintiffs, filed August 19, 1991, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed judgment by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), in which a jury verdict was issued in favor of plaintiff developers on their 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims.. Defense motions for new trial on liability and for judgment, notwithstanding the denial of the verdict, were denied, and plaintiffs were awarded attorney's fees. Plaintiffs cross-appealed the granting of defendant's motion for new trial on damages.


OVERVIEW: As an initial matter, the court determined that the judgment under review was a final judgment, despite the consolidation of causes of action and the severability of certain claims. The court then held that it was error to deny defendant city's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict in favor of plaintiff developers on the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims. The court found that plaintiffs had not shown that they were deprived of a federal constitutional right when plaintiffs were prevented from building the development project in question. The court rejected plaintiffs' assertion that they had a vested right to develop their property, noting that they had not applied for or received a building permit for the project. The court further noted that plaintiffs' substantive due process rights had not been violated where the legislative enactments in question were rationally and substantially related to a legitimate public purpose. The court reversed, declining to reach the issues on cross-appeal where judgment notwithstanding the verdict should have been granted because the rational relation of the zoning actions to legitimate governmental interests was fairly debatable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of plaintiff developers on their federal civil rights claims, reversed the denial of defendant city's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict, and reversed the award of attorney's fees to plaintiffs. Plaintiffs were not entitled to prevail on their civil rights claims where they failed to demonstrate that defendant's zoning actions resulted in a deprivation of plaintiffs' constitutional rights.

 

836 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Godwin


No. B072907.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


31 Cal. App. 4th 1112; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 708; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 51; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 678; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1155



January 24, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 7, 1995. Review Denied May 18, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3386.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA012719, Lance A. Ito, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which was entered following a jury trial in which he was convicted under Cal. Penal Code § 12303.3 of possession of a destructive device or explosive with the intent to injure, intimidate, or terrify any person or with the intent to wrongfully injure or destroy any property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant used a pipe bomb to blow up his van so that he would be able to go to the hospital to recover from his situation. He was charged under § 12303.3; on a jury request for clarification of the word "wrongfully" for purposes of the statute, the judge stated that "wrongful" meant "without legal justification." Defendant was convicted. He claimed that the instruction prejudicially misdirected the jury; he argued that the jury should have been instructed that "wrongfully" required the intent to injure or destroy the property of another or an intent to infringe upon the legal rights of another. The court reversed. The court noted that appellate courts could review any jury instruction, even if no objection was made to it at trial, if a substantial right of defendant was affected. The court held that the judge's instruction permitted the jury to convict defendant if the jury found defendant only intended to injure himself or only intended to injure his own personal property. In fact, the statute did not reach such conduct, which was a matter for other provisions of the California Penal Code.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court committed prejudicial instructional error. The court reversed.

Del Oro Hills v. City of Oceanside


No. D017139.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


31 Cal. App. 4th 1060; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 677; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 50; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 659; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1123



January 23, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 20, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2743.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N37196, Herbert B. Hoffman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent city in an action for declaratory and injunctive relief that Proposition A, City of Oceanside Mun. Code ch. 32A, was invalid and for monetary damages for an alleged unconstitutional taking of property, denial of equal protection, and denial of a vested rights exemption from the proposition.


OVERVIEW: As an initial matter, the appellate court determined that the trial court correctly found that no unconstitutional taking of property occurred through the enactment of Proposition A, City of Oceanside Mun. Code ch. 32A. The appellate court found that the proposition, a growth control regulation, did not, on its face, deny appellant developer all economically viable uses of its property, or fail to advance substantially any legitimate public interest, noting that an invalid regulation was not necessarily an unconstitutional one. The appellate court further found that summary adjudication of appellant's equal protection claim was proper where the existence of the development agreement provided a rational basis to distinguish between appellant and the other developers for purposes of determining whether any vested rights to development had accrued before the proposition went into effect. The appellate court affirmed, upholding the entry of summary judgment on appellant's vested rights/implied contract claim where there was no express representation by respondent city that the property would have been exempt from future regulations governing the timing of development.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the entry of summary judgment in favor of respondent city on all of appellant developer's claims, finding that appellant lacked a viable unconstitutional takings claim where it failed to show that the mere enactment of the invalid growth control ordinance in question denied appellant all economically viable uses of its property or that it did not advance substantially any legitimate public interest.

Green Party of California v. Jones


No. C013273.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


31 Cal. App. 4th 747; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 406; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 38; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 544; 95 Daily Journal DAR 890



January 19, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 23, 1994. Review Denied April 13, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2631.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 369318, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and the ensuing peremptory writ of mandate are modified to strike the provisions compelling the Secretary of State to abide by the Green Party primary closure rules and the choice of none-of-the-above remedy. As so modified the judgment is affirmed. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant secretary of state challenged the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) granting a peremptory writ of mandate requiring appellant to conduct respondent Green Party's primary elections in accordance with rules drafted by respondent rather than the provisions of the California Elections Code.


OVERVIEW: The court modified the trial court's ruling, striking its orders that appellant secretary of state conduct respondent Green Party's primary elections in accordance with two rules adopted by respondent. The first rule required appellant to allow respondent's candidates to run only in races designated by respondent; the second, as modified by the trial court, required appellant to deny certification to any nominee who received less votes than were cast for the ballot category "none of the above." Although the court found that requiring respondent to conduct its elections under the California Elections Code rather than its own rules would affect respondent's associational rights under U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, it wrote that respondent's rules, which were intended to prevent giving a factional minority control of access to its ballot, would not necessarily accomplish this goal. Given this and appellant's interest in preventing voter confusion and minimizing administrative burdens by having uniform election rules, the court concluded that the restrictions placed upon respondent's associational rights were not severe, and therefore did not violate the Constitution.


OUTCOME: Finding that respondent Green Party's associational rights would not be violated by requiring it to conduct primary elections in accordance with the California Elections Code, the court affirmed the trial court's order, but modified it by striking provisions requiring appellant secretary of state to abide by election rules drafted by respondent.

Los Angeles National Bank v. Bank of Canton


No. B069835.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


31 Cal. App. 4th 726; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 389; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 40; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 535; 95 Daily Journal DAR 910; 25 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. 2d (Callaghan) 873



January 19, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied April 13, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2620.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C618399, Eric E. Younger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The matter is remanded to the superior court for recalculation of prejudgment interest at the rate of 7 percent instead of 10 percent as to each cause of action but otherwise is affirmed. Inasmuch as this recalculation appears to be a purely ministerial act, the parties are invited to stipulate to the proper amount of prejudgment interest. Respondent LANB is awarded its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from summary judgment for respondent in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in a claim under the midnight deadline rule of U.C.C. § 4302.


OVERVIEW: In a check kiting scheme, numerous worthless checks written on an account at appellant bank were cashed at a branch office of respondent bank. After the Federal Reserve released the checks for pickup by appellant's courier, appellant did not notify respondent that it would not honor the checks until after midnight of the following banking day. Respondent filed an action to recover the face amount of the checks under the midnight deadline rule of U.C.C. § 4302. The trial court granted summary judgment to respondent. The appellate court affirmed because strict liability applies where a bank fails to comply with the midnight deadline rule. The court remanded for recalculation of prejudgment interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment because strict liability applies where a bank fails to comply with the midnight deadline rule, and the court remanded for recalculation of prejudgment interest.

People v. Teroganesian


No. B077974.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


31 Cal. App. 4th 1534; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 489; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 82; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 904



January 18, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published February 2, 1995. Review Denied May 18, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3390.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA067619, Jan A. Pluim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The conviction for exploding a destructive device causing bodily injury (count 3) is reversed; defendant's sentence is vacated and the matter is remanded for resentencing on counts 2 and 4.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicted defendant of use of a destructive device and explosive to destroy property, in violation of Cal Penal Code § 12303.3, explosion of a destructive device causing bodily injury in violation of § 12309, and first degree burglary in violation of § 459. Defendant challenged the trial court's decision.


OVERVIEW: Defendant placed a pipe bomb beneath a motorist's car. The motorist did not know defendant and could not think of a reason why defendant would place a bomb under the automobile. The bomb detonated while defendant placed it under the car, severely injuring defendant. According to defendant, he had not placed the bomb in the vehicle, but was in the process of stealing the vehicle when the explosion occurred. The trial court convicted defendant of three separate offenses. On appeal, defendant argued that he could not be convicted under Cal. Penal Code § 12309 when no victim was injured when the car bomb detonated. The court, agreeing with defendant, held that the crime defined by § 12309 required the infliction of injury upon another person. Moreover, the court held that there was no logical explanation to punish defendant with life in prison for causing greater bodily injury to himself.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision, which convicted defendant of exploding a destructive device causing bodily injury. Moreover, the court vacated defendant's sentence for that offense and remanded the case for sentencing on the two remaining counts.

 

841 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



American Assn. of Women v. Board of Trustees


No. B072439.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


31 Cal. App. 4th 702; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 15; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 32; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 455; 95 Daily Journal DAR 725



January 17, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC061221, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting peremptory writ of mandate and preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state university and intervenor alien student proponent sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff women's association's petition for writ of mandate compelling defendant to comply with Cal. Educ. Code § 68062(h).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff women's association filed a petition for mandamus and injunctive relief to compel defendant state university to follow Cal. Educ. Code § 68062(h), which precluded undocumented alien students from qualifying as residents of California for tuition purposes. Five years prior, in another county, intervenor alien student proponent successfully sued to enjoin another state university from applying the same code section. An appellate court later upheld the validity of the code section. The court from the other county distinguished its prior order and continued to enforce its injunction despite the appellate court's ruling. In plaintiff's suit, intervenor filed demurrers, motions to strike, and motions to transfer the cause to the other county. The trial court denied the motions and issued a writ of mandate compelling defendant to comply with the code section. The court held that Cal. Const. art. III, § 3.5 was satisfied because an appellate court had determined that the code section was valid on its face and had interpreted the section. The court held that the prior appellate decision was binding on defendant. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement of the trial court because the validity of the statute in question was correctly determined in a prior appellate court decision.

 

842 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (romero)


No. D022175.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


44 Cal. App. 4th 1073; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 364; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 30; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 463



January 13, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted April 13, 1995 (S045097), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2601.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 31 Cal. App. 4th 653 and 35 Cal. App. 4th 1313 and 40 Cal. App. 4th 183.)] (See 13 Cal. 4th 497)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. SCD 103345, William D. Mudd, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to vacate the sentence imposed and to reinstate the "life-prior" allegation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which, on the trial court's motion following a plea bargain between defendant and the court, struck allegations of prior serious felony convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of cocaine. Defendant was also charged with prior convictions for the same crime and for other serious offenses, which subjected him to a possible indeterminate sentence of 25 years to life under Cal. Penal Code § 667, the three strikes law. Pursuant to a plea bargain with the trial court and over the people's objection, the trial court struck the prior serious offenses and sentenced defendant to a limited jail term. The trial court determined that the three strikes law that prohibited the trial court from striking the offenses violated the doctrine of separation of powers. The trial court found that imposition of an indeterminate sentence for the current drug offense under three strikes constituted cruel or unusual punishment. On appeal, the court vacated the sentence and held that the three strikes limitation on striking allegations of prior convictions did not violate the separation of powers doctrine because the legislation restricted both the ability of both the prosecution and the court to amend the charges. The court further held that imposition of an indeterminate sentence for recidivism did not violate the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate directing the trial court to vacate the sentence imposed and to reinstate the allegations of prior serious offenses. The court held that the prohibition of three strikes law against striking allegations of prior serious convictions did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers and that the imposition of an indeterminate sentence under three strikes legislation did constitute cruel or unusual punishment.

 

843 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. One 1986 Toyota Pickup


No. F020303.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


31 Cal. App. 4th 254; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 29; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 2; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 160



January 3, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 220369, James M. Stuart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant appealed the default judgment entered by the Superior Court, Kern County (California) in the state's action which had sought forfeiture of claimant's pickup truck pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11488.5(a)(2) and the order denying claimant relief from the default judgment.


OVERVIEW: The state mailed a personal notice of nonjudicial forfeiture to claimant, the registered owner of a pickup truck that was seized when the driver of the truck was arrested for possession of marijuana. Claimant filed a claim with the lower court to oppose the forfeiture. Some five months later, the state filed a complaint for forfeiture of the truck. In another five months, the state moved for default judgment of the truck on the basis that no answer had been filed in opposition to the complaint. On the date set for proving the default judgment, the lower court did not allow claimant to appear, ruling that it was a default proceeding, and claimant's failure to file an answer precluded his right to appear. The lower court entered a default judgment. Claimant later moved for relief from the default judgment, which was denied by the lower court, and claimant appealed. The court found the lower court's failure to allow claimant to appear and be heard at the date set for proving up the default was an abuse of discretion. No default had been entered against claimant prior to the date of the hearing; thus, his ability to file an answer, appear, or make some other motion was not cut off.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the default judgment entered by the lower court because the lower court's failure to allow claimant to appear and be heard absent an entry of default was an abuse of discretion. The matter was remanded to the lower court with instructions to grant claimant 30 days' leave to file an answer to the complaint for forfeiture.

 

844 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Cody W.


No. G015365.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


31 Cal. App. 4th 221; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 848; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1312; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 157



December 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-424867, Gary Bischoff, Temporary Judge.* *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which terminated her parental rights at a selection and implementation hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother's son was removed from her care and she was incarcerated for much of the next two years. After failing to participate in court-ordered treatment programs, her parental rights were terminated. Upon review, the court affirmed, finding that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 was not unconstitutional as a finding that an award of custody to the parent would be detrimental to the child before terminating parental rights was the equivalent of a finding of unfitness. The court found that there was no equal protection violation because birth parents whose rights were terminated under § 366.26, were not similarly situated to birth parents whose rights were terminated under Cal. Fam. Code § 7828. The court held that "detriment" in the dependency scheme meant weighing all relevant factors that could cause harm to a child as a result of his return to parental custody and that less severe alternatives meant giving a birth parent the opportunity to rehabilitate herself and reunite with her family via court-ordered services before termination of their rights. The court concluded that reasonable reunification services were provided and appellant did not participate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that terminated the parental rights of appellant mother. The court found that the termination of appellant mother's parental rights did not violate due process or equal protection principles and that termination was proper because appellant did not participate in reasonable reunification services or court-ordered rehabilitation.

 

845 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Eid


No. B076587.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


31 Cal. App. 4th 114; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 835; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1306; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 23; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8



December 29, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 12, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2470.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. GA012274, Terry Lee Smerling, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed the information filed against defendants that alleged various sexual offenses and attempted offenses perpetrated on an unconscious victim.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were accused of perpetrating sexual offenses and attempted offenses on an unconscious victim. At their preliminary hearing, the magistrate denied their request to call the victim as a witness as they had failed to make a sufficient offer of proof pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 866(a). The trial court found that the magistrate abused his discretion and dismissed the case. The state appealed. Upon review, the court reversed, finding that a valid offer of proof should have evidence that was attributed to the testimony of specific witnesses, writings, material objects, or other things presented to the senses, that proved the existence or nonexistence of a fact in issue. The court held that defendants' expectation that the victim might retract her statements was not sufficient under § 866(a). The court found that defendants' right to confrontation was not violated, as it did not attach until the trial portion of the proceedings. The court held that preliminary hearings were limited to establishing probable cause only, and were not to be used for discovery. As the defendants were not legally committed, the court ordered the dismissal to be set aside.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, finding that defendants' expectation that the victim might retract her statements was not a sufficient offer of proof to justify her cross-examination in a preliminary hearing that was limited to establishing probable cause. The court ordered the dismissal to be set aside, as the defendants had not been legally committed.

 

846 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Eastern Mun. Water Dist. v. City of Moreno Valley


No. E013587.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


31 Cal. App. 4th 24; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 823; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1296; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9841; 94 Daily Journal DAR 18235



December 27, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 30, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2214.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 214788, Victor Miceli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs on appeal to be borne by appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant water district challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent city after refusing to enjoin enforcement of a local ordinance enacted by respondent. Appellant argued that respondent lacked the statutory authority to require appellant to collect taxes and remit them to respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant water district filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief seeking a judicial determination that respondent city's ordinances were invalid to the extent they required appellant to collect utility users' taxes. Several reasons were listed as to why the tax was invalid including that it constituted an unlawful interference, the collection duty was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. XVI, § 6, and respondent had no legal authority to require appellant to collect the tax. The trial court entered judgment for respondent and found that respondent could validly require appellant to collect the taxes. On review, appellant argued that respondent lacked statutory authority to require appellant to collect the tax and the absence of that authority made the requirement invalid. The court affirmed and found that Cal. Gov't Code § 37100.5 authorized both the tax and reasonable means for its collection, which could include requiring appellant to collect the tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment validating the collection of utility users' taxes. The court held that the requirement that appellant water district collect the tax was authorized as a reasonable means of collection by the same statute that gave respondent city the authority to impose the tax.

People v. Carson


No. D019199, No. D021379.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


39 Cal. App. 4th 1713; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 790; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1287; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9729; 94 Daily Journal DAR 18066



December 22, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 16, 1995 (S044349), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 1962.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 30 Cal. App. 4th 1810 and 35 Cal. App. 4th 432.)]
Review dismissed January 18, 1996 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 4th Dist., Div. 1 (rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1996 Cal. LEXIS 368.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR137285, Bernard E. Revak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed and the petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), convicting him of lewd and lascivious conduct on the body of a child 14 or 15 years of age, Cal. Penal Code § 288(c), and entering a guilty plea to one count of forcible lewd and lascivious conduct on the body of a child under the age of 14, Cal. Penal Code § 288(b). Defendant also brought a petition for a writ of mandate challenging his plea of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of forcible lewd and lascivious conduct on the body of a child under the age of 14, Cal. Penal Code § 288(c), and he pleaded guilty to forcible lewd and lascivious conduct on the body of a child under the age of 14, Cal. Penal Code § 288(b). Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in instructing the jury by defining lewd and lascivious act as any touching with the required intent, and by defining reasonable doubt pursuant to Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 2.90, and that his motion to dismiss based on pre-information delay was improperly denied. Defendant also brought a petition for a writ of mandate challenging the voluntariness of his guilty plea. The court ruled that the standard jury instructions defining a lewd or lascivious act and reasonable doubt were proper. Further, the court held that no reversal was required on the basis that defendant was denied his right to a speedy trial. The court affirmed the judgment and denied the petition, concluding that defendant was given all the necessary advisements before he pleaded guilty.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting defendant was affirmed, and the petition for a writ of mandate was denied, where the trial court properly instructed the jury on the definition of a lewd and lascivious act, defendant was not prejudiced by any pre-information delay, and defendant entered his guilty plea fully understanding the consequences of the plea.

 

848 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Forrester


No. C016666.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


30 Cal. App. 4th 1697; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 19; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1278; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9675; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17915



December 20, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* See footnote 1, post, page 1700.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied March 29, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2271.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Calaveras County, No. 3106, Orrin K. Airola, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction from the Superior Court of Calaveras County (California) for second degree burglary under Cal. Pen. Code §§ 459, 460, and failure to appear pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 1320 (b), contending that jury instructions violated his presumption of innocence, and invaded the province of the jury.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant testified that he had failed to appear in court to face charges of second degree burglary on two dates, first because his car had broken down, and second because he felt that he had done nothing wrong, and therefore did not need to appear. The trial judge instructed the jury that it was presumed that a defendant who willfully failed to appear within 14 days of the date of his appearance had intended to evade the process of the court. On appeal, defendant contended that the instruction violated his presumption of innocence and the province of the jury. The court held that specific intent to evade the process of the court was an element of the offense under Cal. Penal Code § 1320, and the instruction, which included the presumption of willful evasion relieved the prosecution of proving that element, merely by establishing the predicate facts. The court affirmed, reasoning that the error did not prejudice defendant because no rational trier of fact could have concluded from the facts that defendant did not intend to evade process, and held that the instruction did not violate defendant's rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for second degree burglary and failure to appear, holding that while the jury instruction was constitutionally infirm, the instruction did not prejudice defendant.

 

849 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Susan H. v. Jack S.


No. B075767.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


30 Cal. App. 4th 1435; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 120; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1280; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9622; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17764



December 19, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BF002634, Keith M. Clemens, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which applied the presumption of former Cal. Evid. Code § 621 (now Cal. Fam. Code §§ 7540, 7541) and granted summary judgment in favor of respondent putative father. The issue was whether the presumption should have been applied in favor of respondent, who denied paternity.


OVERVIEW: While married to another man, appellant mother conceived a child with respondent putative father. The marriage continued, and the husband and child became attached to each other. Appellant and her husband divorced, and the husband was given extensive custody rights. Appellant then filed a paternity action against respondent. He moved for summary judgment and appellant provided no controverting evidence. The lower court applied the presumption of former Cal. Evid. Code § 621 (now Cal. Fam. Code §§ 7549, 7541) and granted summary judgment for respondent. On appeal, the court noted that the presumption was a rule of substantive law. Appellant's husband was deemed the child's father, because the child was conceived during their marriage. The presumption was based on, among other things, the welfare of the child and the state's interest in protecting the integrity of the family unit. Appellant had not demonstrated any facts to show that the child would be better off if respondent was legally his father. Parental rights went beyond a biological connection. The facts did not establish a contrary presumption. The statute was constitutional and the court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment to respondent putative father. The court held that the undisputed facts supported application of the presumption against appellant mother that the child's father was appellant's ex-husband, because she was married when the child was conceived. Facts to support a contrary presumption were not present in the evidence.

 

850 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Tuffli v. Governing Board


No. D018764.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


30 Cal. App. 4th 1398; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 433; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1309



December 15, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 657054, Kevin W. Midlam, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to grant the petition to the extent of requiring the District to conduct proceedings to determine if a dismissal for cause is warranted, and to dispose of any backpay issues concerning the time period since Tuffli's credential was reissued.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher challenged a decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate seeking an order compelling defendant school district to reinstate him as teacher with back pay. Plaintiff contended that summary termination by defendant pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 44836, was violative of statutory and constitutional protections of his property interest in continued employment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff teacher, convicted of committing a sex offense with a mentally handicapped pupil, had his credentials suspended by defendant school district pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code §§ 44360(e), 44940(d), and he was terminated from employment. Plaintiff appealed his conviction and the court reversed the judgment. Plaintiff's credentials were reissued and he requested that defendant reinstate him, but his request was denied. Plaintiff filed a petition for writ of mandate, seeking an order compelling defendant to reinstate him with back pay, but the writ was denied. Plaintiff appealed, contending that summary termination by defendant, pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 44836, was a violation of the statutory and constitutional protections of his property interest in continued employment. The court reversed, directing the trial court to order defendant to hold a hearing on cause for dismissal and to make a determination on the back pay issues because plaintiff's credentials were reissued. The court stated that plaintiff became eligible for continued employment and was entitled to protections of dismissal for cause proceedings when the predicate for his summary dismissal no longer existed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of the petition, and granted plaintiff teacher's writ of mandate. The court directed the trial court to order defendant school district to hold a hearing on cause for dismissal and to make a determination on the back pay issues because plaintiff's credentials were reissued after his conviction for a sex offense was reversed and charges dismissed.

 

851 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Curtis


No. E012534.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


30 Cal. App. 4th 1337; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 304; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1259; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9504; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17567



December 14, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts VI, VII, and VIII.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied March 23, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2173.
Review denied by People v. Curtis, 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2173 (Cal., Mar. 23, 1995)
Writ of certiorari denied Curtis v. California, 516 U.S. 948, 116 S. Ct. 391, 133 L. Ed. 2d 312, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 7298 (1995)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Curtis v. Dickinson, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 78452 (C.D. Cal., June 8, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-40945, W. Charles Morgan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant disputed a conviction for second degree murder in the Superior Court of Riverside County (California). He argued that a jury instruction on implied malice was erroneous, and instructions on self-defense, defense of habitation, and imperfect self-defense should have been given, because his evidence disproved implied malice and there had been instructions on excusable homicide, involuntary manslaughter, and brandishing a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed his girlfriend as her brother was trying to get into his apartment. Defendant claimed that the gun went off accidentally. He was convicted of second degree murder, and he appealed, asserting erroneous jury instructions. The court held that there was no error in instructing the jury on implied malice using the "conscious disregard" definition in Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 8.11 (5th ed. 1988), rather than the "wanton disregard" definition. The instruction adequately covered the concept that malice required a high probability that defendant's act would result in death, and the "base, antisocial motive" element was not necessary. Instructions on imperfect self-defense, traditional self-defense, and defense of habitation were not required. They were inconsistent with defendant's claim that the gun went off accidentally. Likewise, an instruction on self-defense on the brandishing charge was not necessary, and any error was harmless. There was no evidence that anything would have led defendant to believe that he had to defend his home. He was not entitled to a statutory presumption, because there was no actual entry. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction for second degree murder. It was not error to instruct on implied malice using the conscious disregard definition where it fully informed the jury. Instructions on imperfect self-defense, traditional self-defense, and defense of habitation were not required. They were inconsistent with defendant's accidental murder claim. Also, he could not have believed that someone was trying to break into his home.

 

852 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



California Assn. for Safety Education v. Brown


No. H011544.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


30 Cal. App. 4th 1264; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 404; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1252; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9475; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17504



December 13, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 23, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2176.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 96920, Richard M. Silver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We hold that high school districts violate the free school clause when they charge students for driver training. We also hold that DTPAF funds may be used to pay for driver training only if there is an appropriation in the annual Budget Act. (§ 41305.) We affirm the trial court's denial of appellants' petition for a writ of mandamus and preliminary injunction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state association for safety education and school district, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), that denied appellants' petition for writ of mandamus and preliminary injunction. Appellants' action was brought against respondent state treasurer to pay for driver training for public high school students, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1464 and Cal. Educ. Code § 41304.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, state association for safety education and school district, brought an action against respondent state treasurer to pay for driver training for public high school students. Appellants claimed that moneys from the Drivers Training Penalty Assessment Fund (DTPAF) should have been used for driver training, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1464 and Cal. Educ. Code § 41304. The trial court denied appellants' petition for a writ of mandamus and preliminary injunction. The appeals court affirmed, holding that although fees charged by a high school district for driver training violated the free school guarantee of Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5, DTPAF funds could be used to pay for driver training only if there was an appropriation in the annual Budget Act. The court held that respondent did not breach its contractual obligation to reimburse appellants for driver training expenses, the DTPAF did not constitute a trust fund, and respondent did not violate the equal protection clause, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The contentions in the amici curiae were not considered. The trial court's denials of appellants' petition for a writ of mandamus and preliminary injunction were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the superior court that denied appellants', state association for safety education and school district, petition for writ of mandamus and preliminary injunction against respondent state treasurer, holding that although fees charged by a high school district for driver training violated the constitutional free school guarantee, the funds could be used only if there was an appropriation in the annual Budget Act.

 

853 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Gensburg v. Miller


No. A063468.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


31 Cal. App. 4th 512; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 97; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1320; 95 Daily Journal DAR 527



December 12, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published January 11, 1995 on Denial of Rehearing, Reported at: 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 21.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 377576, Harlan K. Veal, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foster parents sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California), which sustained respondent state department of social services (DDS), county DDS, and county's' demurrer without leave to amend on the ground that respondents were absolutely immune from civil liability for their alleged misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellant foster parents operated a foster home out of their residence in the county. In a federal civil rights action against respondents, state department of social services (DSS), county DSS, and county, appellants contended that their foster home license was wrongfully suspended. The court held that any state or common-law actions against respondents were barred under Cal. Gov't Code § 821.6. Furthermore, the court held that respondents were entitled to absolute immunity against 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims. Where respondents filed an accusation against appellants and instituted suspension proceedings, the court held that respondents were acting in a prosecutorial role in administrative proceedings and, thus, were entitled to absolute immunity under section 1983. Finally, the court held that in implementing disciplinary action to protect the welfare of foster children under appellants' care, respondents were acting in a prosecutorial role; therefore, respondents were entitled to absolute immunity for their actions causing proceedings to be initiated against appellants. As such, the court affirmed the judgment that sustained respondents' demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sustaining a demurrer by respondent, state department of social services (DDS), county DDS, and county, holding that respondents were absolutely immune to appellant foster parents' federal civil rights action because respondents' role in instituting suspension proceedings and implementing disciplinary action against appellants was prosecutorial in nature.

 

854 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Castro v. Higaki


No. A062161.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


31 Cal. App. 4th 350; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 84; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1316; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 207; 95 Daily Journal DAR 325



December 6, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published January 5, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 942589, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant disputed a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed her complaint under the doctrine of res judicata, which had alleged conspiracy between by respondent attorney and another to hide assets. She argued that her claim was not barred by res judicata where dismissal of a prior complaint was not on the merits and the cases did not involve the same primary right.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant filed a petition under Cal. Civ. Code § 1714.10, alleging a conspiracy between respondent attorney and another to hide a client's assets. The petition was dismissed. She filed a second petition after the statute was amended. The trial court dismissed without leave to amend. On appeal, the court held that the first dismissal was a final, appealable order. Her second petition was virtually identical to the first petition, so it was based on the same primary right. By analogy to the federal summary judgment standard, the first dismissal of her statutory petition was a judgment on the merits. Therefore, her second petition was barred by res judicata. The amendment of the statute did not provide her an opportunity to file a new petition. Appellant's claim that the trial judge had unconstitutionally weighed the facts in his decision on the first petition was an issue which should have been raised by an appeal of that decision. The grounds of the first decision were inconsequential; all that mattered was that the trial judge's order was valid. Appellant had no constitutional right to a jury trial, because the matter was an issue of law. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant claimant petition, where the court found that her first petition was dismissed by a final order on the merits and the instant petition involved the same primary right. The court held that res judicata was properly applied. The court noted that if there was error in the first dismissal, she should have appealed.

 

855 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Dixon v. Superior Court


No. G015646.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


30 Cal. App. 4th 733; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 687; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1223; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9126; 94 Daily Journal DAR 16878; 23 Media L. Rep. 1663



November 30, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 23, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2174.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 719488, Frederick Paul Horn, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner archaeologist filed a petition for writ of mandate which sought to vacate respondent trial court's judgment denying his motion to strike a complaint against him for intentional and negligent interference with contractual relations and prospective economic advantage, libel, slander, and trade libel.


OVERVIEW: A survey company was hired to perform archaeological tests on a portion of an ancient Indian village. The survey company's report proposed that a project to build a museum and a strip mall would cause no adverse effects to the site. Petitioner archaeologist stated that the survey company's report was "poorly done," and began a letter-writing campaign challenging the report's findings. The survey company filed a complaint against petitioner seeking damages for, among other things, libel, slander, and trade libel. Respondent trial court denied petitioner's motion to strike the complaint. The court held that the suit against petitioner was a strategic lawsuit against public participation (SLAPP), as defined under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 425.16(b). Therefore, where petitioner showed that his statements regarding the survey company were made in response to a matter of public concern, petitioner was brought within the protection of section 425.16, which entitled petitioner's statements, even if malicious, to absolute immunity. As such, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to vacate its order denying petitioner's motion to strike the complaint against him.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner archaeologist's motion to strike a complaint against him for libel and slander, holding that petitioner's comments about a report, which stated that building on an ancient Indian village site would cause no adverse effects, were made in response to a matter of public concern and, thus, entitled to absolute immunity under statute.

 

856 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Mitchell


No. G013424.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


30 Cal. App. 4th 783; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 150; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 1234; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9241; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17008



November 30, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts V through IX.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-87342, Richard L. Weatherspoon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged her conviction in the Superior Court of Orange County (California), pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11370.6 for possessing over $ 100,000 obtained from drug sales and conspiracy to commit that crime. Defendant contended that the statute was unconstitutional because it did not criminalize possession of lesser sums.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed her conviction, pursuant to Cal. Health § Safety Code § 11370.6, for possessing over $ 100,000 obtained from drug sales and conspiracy to commit that crime. Defendant was convicted based on evidence that she was in possession of large amounts of cash and that she intended to purchase a plane often used by drug smugglers. The court affirmed, finding that the statute did not violate her rights to equal protection and due process, and was not vague or overly broad. The court was not required to subject all criminal classifications to strict scrutiny requiring the showing of a compelling state interest. The legislature rationally limited culpability to possessors of large amounts of cash where the possessor had knowledge of its illegal source. The court held that the state had a legitimate interest in restricting drug trafficking and the statute furthered that interest. The evidence sufficiently showed that the money in defendant's possession was from unlawful drug activities and that defendant knew it. The court did not err in its instructions to the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of over $ 100,000 obtained from drug sales and conspiracy to commit that crime. The statute under which defendant was charged did not violate her rights to equal protection and due process, and was not overly broad or vague. The evidence was sufficient to prove that the money was from unlawful drug activities and the defendant knew of its illegal source.
