People v. Higareda


No. B064544.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


24 Cal. App. 4th 1399; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 763; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 456; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3263; 94 Daily Journal DAR 6172



May 5, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. GA004753, Gilbert C. Alston and Janice Claire Croft, Judges.



DISPOSITION: As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of nine counts of robbery and one count of discharging a firearm with gross negligence. Defendant was sentenced to a 23-year, 8-month state prison term.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of nine counts of robbery and one count of discharging a firearm with gross negligence. Defendant was sentenced to a 23-year, 8-month state prison term. Upon appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held a special instruction regarding force and fear was improper because it established elements of the offense of robbery and that was the role of the jury. The court found, however, the error was nonreversible where neither force nor fear were at issue and the jury could not have reasonably concluded force and fear were not established where they found defendant aimed a gun at a victim in demand for money. The court held defendant's confession was not rendered involuntary by the investigator telling him he would talk to the district attorney if defendant told the truth because there was no actual threat or promise. The court held that under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code 1731.5, the trial court was not bound by a Youth Authority housing recommendation. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 654 was not violated where defendant was punished separately for firing a shotgun where the violent offenses involved different victims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted defendant of nine counts of robbery and one count of discharging a firearm with gross negligence. The court held a special instruction pertaining to force and fear was improper because it established elements of the offense of robbery and it was the role of the jury to make the determinations. The court held the error was not reversible because the elements of force and fear were not at issue.

 

912 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Littoral Development Co. v. San Francisco Bay Conservation Etc. Com.


No. A061428.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


24 Cal. App. 4th 1050; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 518; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 429; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3140; 94 Daily Journal DAR 6006



May 2, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing May 26, 1994


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 148079, Gary W. Thomas, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant land owner challenged the denial of its petition for writ of mandate by the Superior Court of Marin County (California), in an action seeking to overturn the decision of respondent conservation and development commission which determined it had jurisdiction over appellant's land.


OVERVIEW: Appellant land owner sought to construct a hotel on its property. Respondent conservation and development commission claimed jurisdiction over the majority of appellant's property. The court held that the plain language of Cal. Gov't Code § 66610 (1969) stated that, except as to marshlands, the shoreline of the bay was to be set with reference to the specified level of "mean high tide" and not, as respondent contended, at the line of maximum inundation by occasional and exceptional high tides. The court noted that this measure of tidelands had been consistently adopted by California law. Consequently, the court held that the landward two-thirds of appellant's property, which was not shown to lie less than 100 feet beyond the level of mean high tide, was not within respondent's jurisdiction. However, the bayward one-third was marshland lying lower than five feet above mean sea level and did come within respondent's jurisdiction. The court reversed that portion of the judgment establishing respondent's jurisdiction at the record high tide level, affirmed its jurisdiction as to the marshland portion of appellant's land, and remanded the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant land owner's petition for writ of mandate with respect to the establishment of respondent conservation and development commission's jurisdiction over property because the statute authorized jurisdiction at the "mean high tide" level. The court affirmed the denial with respect to the marshland portion because that was clearly within respondent's jurisdiction and remanded.

Smith v. County of Los Angeles


No. B072488.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


24 Cal. App. 4th 990; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 680; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 436; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3167; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5909



April 29, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 26, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. LASC B072488, Stephen E. O'Neil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with instructions to issue the peremptory writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant business owner challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for mandamus relief in his action challenging the denial of a conditional use permit for his adult business by respondent county under Los Angeles County Code § 22.56.190.


OVERVIEW: Appellant business owner applied for a conditional use permit (CUP) to open an adult business. Respondent county denied the permit under Los Angeles County Code § 22.56.190, which prohibited adult businesses within 500 feet of certain properties. Appellant sought mandamus relief from the trial court, which was denied. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and was a prior restraint on appellant's freedoms under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court found that the ordinance delegated subjective discretion to county officials without setting forth narrow, objective, and definite standards for denying CUP's to adult businesses, which were to be focussed on the secondary effect of such businesses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant business owner's petition for mandamus relief, holding that the Los Angeles County ordinance was unconstitutional as being a prior restraint and being vague because it did not set forth narrow, objective, and definite standards.

Brumer v. City of Los Angeles


No. B066496.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


24 Cal. App. 4th 983; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 515; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 441; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3151; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5877



April 29, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC 001773, Paul Boland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs to respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents of decedent sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent city on the ground of statutory immunity for injuries resulting from police pursuits pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 17004.7, in appellants' wrongful death action for their son's death in a crash resulting from a police pursuit.


OVERVIEW: Appellants parents of decedent whose son died as the result of a police pursuit, challenged the trial court's grant of summary judgment to respondent city based on respondent 's immunity from injuries arising out of vehicle pursuits under Cal. Veh. Code § 17004.7. Appellants contended that respondent's vehicle pursuit policy did not qualify respondent for immunity under § 17004.7, that the policy was illusory, and that the policy's guidelines did not satisfy Cal. Veh. Code § 17004.7). The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that respondent's policy qualified for immunity under § 17004.7 because it was promulgated by the chief of police as head of a public agency, and that adoption of the policy in accordance with the statutory requirements triggered the immunity, regardless of the policy's actual implementation. The court further held that the policy did not leave officers with unfettered discretion, and that respondent did not waive the immunity defense despite its failure to cite to § 17004.7 in its answer. Thus, respondent's vehicle pursuit policy complied with § 17004.7 in all respects.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting summary judgment in favor of respondent city in wrongful death action by appellant parents of decedent, because respondent's vehicle pursuit policy satisfied all immunity statute requirements, the policy was promulgated by the chief of police, the policy did not leave officers with unfettered discretion, and respondent successfully pled the immunity defense.

Larsen v. Department of Motor Vehicles


No. G013735.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


39 Cal. App. 4th 406; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 636; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 416; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3029; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5736



April 28, 1994, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 12, 1994. Review Granted July 18, 1994 (S040219), Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3946.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 24 Cal. App. 4th 918 and 29 Cal. App. 4th 952 and 34 Cal. App. 4th 799.)] (See 12 Cal. 4th 278)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 69-76-90, Eleanor M. Palk, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to proceed in accordance with the views expressed above.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant motorist sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied his petition in mandamus against respondent motor vehicles department. Appellant sought to challenge the constitutionality of his sister-state conviction for driving while impaired, which resulted in respondent's suspension of his driving privileges pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(d).


OVERVIEW: After appellant motorist was convicted in another state of driving while impaired, respondent motor vehicles department suspended appellant's driving privileges pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(d). Appellant petitioned for a writ of mandamus, asserting that the sister-state conviction was unconstitutional. The lower court denied appellant's petition. The court reversed, holding that as a matter of federal constitutional law, California could not deny a citizen the privilege to drive based on an unconstitutional conviction. The court found that some forum must have been provided to attack an unconstitutional conviction. The court concluded that when there was no other California procedure available, the lower court must have provided a forum to resolve attacks on invalid convictions and prevent their use by respondent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's denial of appellant motorist's petition for a writ of mandamus. It held that as a matter of federal constitutional law, respondent motor vehicles department could not deny a citizen the privilege to drive based on an unconstitutional conviction. The court concluded that the lower court must have provided a forum to resolve attacks on invalid convictions and prevent their use by respondent.

Xiloj-Itzep v. City of Agoura Hills


No. B067188.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


24 Cal. App. 4th 620; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 879; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 424; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3114; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5827



April 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. LC011284, Richard G. Kolostian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs of appeal are awarded to respondents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their request for a preliminary injunction against respondent city in their action to challenge the validity of a city ordinance designed to prohibit vehicle-addressed solicitation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant citizens gathered on the streets of respondent city seeking day work. In response, the city passed an ordinance prohibiting vehicle-addressed solicitation. Some appellants were arrested under the ordinance, and all appellants sought a preliminary injunction against the enforcement of the ordinance. The trial court denied the injunction, and appellants sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's decision. Because the ordinance was directed only at vehicle-addressed solicitation on city streets, it was a reasonable time, place, and manner restriction. The court also held that the ordinance was not overbroad or vague. The court ruled that a trial court could not grant a preliminary injunction against the enforcement of a facially valid ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant citizens' request for a preliminary injunction, holding that a preliminary injunction could not be granted because respondent city's ordinance was facially valid as a reasonable time, place, and manner restriction on vehicle-addressed solicitation.

People v. Griffin


No. C016151.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


24 Cal. App. 4th 1814; 30 Cal. Rptr. 2d 292; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 497; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3705; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3895; 94 Daily Journal DAR 6915



April 26, 1994, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 26, 1994. The Publication Status of the Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published May 26, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CR115423, Peter N. Mering, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted defendant of attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 664(1), possession by a convicted felon of a firearm under Cal. Penal Code § 12021, and discharge of a firearm at an occupied automobile under Cal. Penal Code § 246, and found that the attempted murder was deliberate and premeditated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 664(1) that was deliberate and premeditated. The jury also convicted defendant of possession by a convicted felon of a firearm under Cal. Penal Code § 12021, and discharge of a firearm at an occupied automobile under Cal. Penal Code § 246. The court affirmed the conviction and found that although the information charged defendant with attempted murder, it did not allege that the murder was deliberate and premeditated. However, the court held that the failure of the information to allege separately that the crime was deliberate and premeditated was a "formal" defect and that defendant raised no persuasive claim of prejudice, and thus it was harmless error because defendant was aware of the "lying in wait" theory of the prosecution, the classic theory of premeditation. The court found that the trial court was proper when it sent the jury back to continue deliberations on premeditation after it returned a verdict of first degree attempted murder, because premeditation was encompassed within the attempted murder charged, and the prosecution argued premeditation to the jury without objection by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for deliberate and premeditated attempted murder and held that it was harmless error for the information which charged defendant to not specify premeditation because, defendant was aware of the prosecution's argument that defendant was lying in wait, which was the classic premeditation theory.

Current, Inc. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. A061750.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


24 Cal. App. 4th 382; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 407; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 349; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2846; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5401



April 22, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 938248, John E. Dearman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of equalization sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found a use tax imposed upon respondent company under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6203(g) was unconstitutional as applied to respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent company, an out-of-state mail-order business, was acquired by a corporation with considerable contacts within California. Appellant board of equalization imposed a use tax upon respondent, relying on Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6203(g), which imposed collection responsibility upon any retailer controlled by the same interests that controlled any retailer engaged in business in the same or similar line of business in the state. Appellant challenged the lower court's ruling that the use tax was unconstitutional as applied to respondent. The court affirmed the lower court's decision, after evaluating whether the acquisition of respondent by a corporation created a substantial nexus between respondent and the taxing state of California. The court found that respondent and its acquiring corporation were organized and operated as separate and distinct corporate entities, and neither was the agent of the other for any purpose. The court ruled, therefore, that respondent's physical nexus with the state was insufficient to justify the imposition of a use tax, and that §6203(g), as applied to respondent, was unconstitutional as violative of the commerce clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's finding that the tax statute was unconstitutional as violative of the commerce clause as applied to respondent company because respondent and its parent company were organized as separate corporate entities, and respondent's physical nexus with the state was insufficient to justify the imposition of a use tax.

Branson v. Sun-Diamond Growers


No. C013859.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


24 Cal. App. 4th 327; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 314; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 343; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2789; 9 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 827; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5358



April 21, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 214012, K. Peter Saiers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to set aside its order sustaining Sun-Diamond's demurrer without leave to amend to the second, third, sixth and seventh causes of action of Branson's second amended complaint, and enter a new and different order overruling the demurrer to those causes of action. Branson shall recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff agent appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which granted defendant principal's motion to dismiss on the ground that all causes of action were barred by the principles of res judicata and collateral estoppel in an action for indemnification of an adverse judgment under Cal. Corp. Code § 317(e)(4).


OVERVIEW: Following an adverse judgment, plaintiff agent sought indemnity from defendant principal under Cal. Corp. Code § 317(e)(4). While defendant appealed the order granting indemnification, plaintiff filed an amended complaint for breach of contract theories and for equitable estoppel. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend on the ground that there was another action pending on the same cause of action. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that the trial court should have decided the rights of the parties on the issues in the second action because the prior litigation had not been determined on the merits. The court also held that the prior corporate agency indemnity action under § 317(e)(4) did not have res judicata or collateral estoppel effect because the primary right to seek authorization for indemnity under § 317(e)(4) was not the same as the actions for breach of a contract for indemnity or under the doctrine of equitable estoppel and because the doctrine of collateral estoppel did not apply where the issues relating to contractual indemnity and equitable estoppel were not before the court in the prior action..


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal in favor of defendant principal and remanded with directions to set aside the order sustaining defendant's demurrer without leave to amend because plaintiff agent's prior causes of action for indemnity under the corporate agency statute were unrelated to the breach of a contract for indemnity causes of action in the second case and did not have res judicata or collateral estoppel effect.

 

920 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Thompson


No. B074314.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


24 Cal. App. 4th 299; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 847; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 338; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5281; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2773



April 20, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied July 14, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3924.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. LASC A648471, Richard P. Kalustian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sentenced him to life without the possibility of parole under Cal. Penal Code § 12310, following remand by the court which affirmed his conviction of first degree murder for causing the death of a child. Defendant claimed that his sentence violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 17, as being cruel and unusual and that the trial court abused its discretion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder for his involvement in throwing a molotov cocktail into a house and thus causing the death of a two year old boy. His judgment was affirmed by the court in a prior proceeding, but the case was remanded to the trial court to chose either a 25-year to life sentence under Cal. Penal Code § 187 or life without possibility of parole under Cal. Penal Code § 12310. The trial court sentenced defendant to the latter. Defendant challenged the sentence as cruel and unusual in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. The court affirmed his sentence and held that the only mitigating circumstance was defendant's youth; however, there was no evidence that defendant was immature and did not foresee the risk he was creating. Further, the legislature determined that the crime carried a heavier sentence than a premeditated killing because of the use of a destructive device that marked defendant as a greater danger to society. The court also found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in selecting life without the possibility of parole because of the extraordinarily dangerous nature of the crime and defendant's level of participation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which imposed the sentence of life without the possibility of parole because defendant's sentence was not cruel and unusual punishment because of the extraordinarily dangerous nature of defendant's crime of throwing a molotov cocktail through a window and thereby causing the death of a child.

Gutierrez v. Superior Court


No. B078255.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


24 Cal. App. 4th 153; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 376; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 328; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2662; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5061



April 15, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 11, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 4366.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. KA016953, Alfonso M. Bazan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue directing respondent court to vacate its August 20, 1993, order and to enter a new and different order denying the prosecution's Motion re Collateral Estoppel.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which barred him from litigating the issues of identity and intent on the grounds that a prior final judgment of conviction for attempted murder collaterally estopped a retrial on those issues.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted murder. When the victim later died, defendant was also charged with murder. The trial court ruled that collateral estoppel barred him from litigating the issues of identity and intent. The court vacated that order, looking to the rulings of other jurisdictions because the California supreme court had not ruled on the issue of collateral estoppel when raised by the prosecution to retry a defendant. The elements of collateral estoppel were met in this case because the issue and parties were identical to that in the former proceeding, the issue was actually litigated and decided, and the decision was final on the merits. However, collateral estoppel did not apply because the defendant's right to present a defense outweighed any other public policy or judicial economy considerations.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order, holding that defendant's right to present a defense outweighed any public policy rationales or the need for judicial economy. Accordingly, collateral estoppel did not apply.

 

922 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Ponderosa Homes, Inc. v. City of San Ramon


No. A060955.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


23 Cal. App. 4th 1761; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 26; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 319; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2587; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4864



April 8, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. C9105569, John F. Van De Poel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted judgment on the pleadings to respondents, city and city council, on the grounds that appellant's causes of action were barred by the 180-day statute of limitations in Cal. Gov't Code § 66020 and the one-year statute of limitations in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340(3).


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation filed a petition and complaint against respondents, city and city council, in which appellant challenged a traffic mitigation fee imposed against appellant's development and alleged a violation of its civil rights, pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The trial court granted respondents' motion for judgment on the pleadings on the grounds that appellant's claims were barred by the 180-day statute of limitations in Cal. Gov't Code § 66020 and the one-year statute of limitations in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340(3). On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the limitations period in § 66020 began to run with the imposition of the fees, which occurred when respondent city first set the fees as a condition on its tentative subdivision map approval, and not when appellant paid its first installment. The court also held that the limitations period in § 340(3), which was applicable to the § 1983 claim, began to run when respondent city inflicted concrete, actual harm. This also occurred when the fees were imposed. Because appellant's petition and complaint were filed over three years after the imposition of the fees, its claims against respondents were time-barred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment on the pleadings granted by the trial court. The court held that the applicable limitations periods began to run when respondent city inflicted actual harm upon appellant corporation, which occurred when respondents, city and city council, imposed fees against appellant's development.

 

923 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Bell v. Board of Supervisors


No. A056574.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


23 Cal. App. 4th 1695; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 919; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 301; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2441; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4631



April 6, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing May 2, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 684766-7, 684954-8, and 685279-5, Demetrios P. Agretelis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That part of the judgment directing issue of a writ of mandate commanding County to refrain from implementing any grant reductions for general assistance recipients who are homeless or whose shelter and utility costs are less than $194 per month is modified to direct issue of a writ of mandate commanding County to refrain from implementing any such grant reductions where in-kind shelter benefits are neither provided to such recipients nor realistically available to them. As modified, that part of the judgment is affirmed. The portion of the judgment awarding attorney fees in an amount to be determined is affirmed, with directions to consider the parties' relative success in this appeal as well as in the trial court. The remainder of the judgment is reversed. The trial court is directed to vacate its writ of mandate and to issue a new writ in conformity with this opinion. Respondents shall be allowed their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county board of supervisors sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate in favor of respondent general assistance recipients, directing appellant to refrain from implementing any grant reductions for homeless general assistance recipients and to refrain from implementing its proposed and existing vendor pay plan.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county board of supervisors adopted an ordinance reducing its general assistance plan to recipients who were homeless or whose shelter and utility costs were under a specified amount, and revising its vendor payment plan for food, transportation, shelter, and utilities. Respondent general assistance recipients challenged the ordinance and were granted a peremptory writ of mandate, which barred appellant's implementation of the changes. Appellant sought review on the grounds that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 17000.5 allowed the changes. The court affirmed in part with modifications, holding that while appellant could reduce shelter benefits by the value of housing provided to respondents, it could not reduce them by the value of shelter benefits not actually received nor available to respondents. Because the benefit reductions in other parts of the program were authorized under the safe harbor provisions of § 17000.5, the court held that appellant was not required to justify those reductions. The court reversed the writ as it applied to the appellant's change to the vendor pay program, finding that those changes were within appellant's discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the writ of mandate in favor of respondent general assistance recipients with modifications, because appellant county board of supervisors' reduction in benefits in its general assistance program was within its discretion, but the reduction for shelter not actually received nor available to recipients was improper. The court reversed the writ as it pertained to appellant's vendor pay program changes.

Transamerica Realty Servs. v. Cal. Coastal Com


No. B078071.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


23 Cal. App. 4th 1536; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 773; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 289; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2378; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4338



March 31, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied June 2, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS022700, Douglas A. McKee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant California Coastal Commission challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate to respondent real estate developer ordering appellant to vacate respondent's coastal development permit, and to dismiss an appeal that challenged the approval of the permit for lack of subject matter jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: City of Rancho Palos Verdes approved respondent real estate developer's application for a coastal development permit, and notified appellant California Coastal Commission. An appeal of the permit decision was filed after the time limit required by Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30603(c), but within the time limit required by appellant's regulation under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 13111(b). Appellant approved the permit, but with conditions unsatisfactory to respondent. Respondent sought a writ of mandate. The trial court granted and ordered appellant to vacate the permit, and dismiss the appeal for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The court affirmed, holding that the § 30603(c) provision allowing appeals to be filed within 10 working days after local government action controlled, and the provision of § 13111(b) allowing appeals to be filed within 10 days of receipt of such notice action was void to the extent that it allowed appeals to be filed outside the time limit. The court also held that § 30603(c) permitted local government to require filing of a notice of final action with appellant, and that it did not prejudice the appeal rights of third parties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of a peremptory writ of mandate to respondent real estate developer, ordered appellant California Coastal Commission to vacate respondent's coastal development permit, and to dismiss an appeal from approval of the permit for lack of subject matter jurisdiction, because the appeal was not filed within the time allowed under the controlling statutory provision, and the regulation expanding the filing time was void.

County of Los Angeles v. Sasaki


No. B077722.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


23 Cal. App. 4th 1442; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 103; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 274; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2253; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4209



March 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC084504, Diane Wayne, Judge. * 


* Retired Associate Justice of the Court of Appeal, Second District, sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant County of Los Angeles appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent county auditor to disburse property tax receipts in accordance with Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance 93-0045, ch. 4.200.


OVERVIEW: Appellant County of Los Angeles enacted the Apportionment of Property Taxes, Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance 93-0045, ch. 4.200 (ordinance), in anticipation of the legislature's passage of Cal. Rev. & Tax Code §§ 97.02, 97.035, which reallocated property tax revenues from counties, cities, and special districts to educational revenue augmentation funds. Appellant sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent county auditor to ignore §§ 97.02 and 97.035, and to disburse property tax receipts in accordance with the ordinance. The court affirmed denial of the writ and held that §§ 97.02 and 97.035 did not violate the home rule provisions of Cal. Const. art. XIII A because appellant's taxing power was neither destroyed nor annulled. The court held that appellant's ordinance was void because it conflicted with proper state law and appellant had not been empowered to enact legislation in this tax area. The court ruled that appellant lacked the standing to challenge the constitutionality of Cal. Ed. Code § 41204.5, and had waived its challenge by voluntarily dismissing its cause of action for declaratory relief and failing to provide a factual record for its challenge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed denial of appellant County of Los Angeles' petition for writ of mandate because it lacked the standing to challenge the constitutionality of the legislature's reallocation of property tax revenue. The court held that appellant failed to establish that the legislature's reallocation violated home rule, that its ordinance was void because it conflicted with state law, and that it lacked the legal authority to enact the ordinance.

Tahoe Keys Property Owners' Assn. v. State Water Resources Control Bd.


No. C012562.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


23 Cal. App. 4th 1459; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 734; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 303; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2451; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4345



March 30, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied June 16, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3274.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of El Dorado County, No. SV91-0164, J. Hilary Cook, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the Alpine Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order denying TKPOA's request for a preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner's association appealed from the denial by the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), of its request for a preliminary injunction against defendants, governmental water and resource agencies, to preclude defendants' collection of further per lot mitigation fees paid for construction permits, and to require collected mitigation fees to be paid over to plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, governmental water and resource agencies, reclassified the area in which members of plaintiff home owner's association lived so that they could obtain building permits upon their paying a mitigation fee which would be used to accomplish relevant water control plans. Plaintiff sought to permanently enjoin further collection of the mitigation fee and for a refund of collected fees. Plaintiff appealed the denial of its request for a preliminary injunction. The court affirmed, holding that plaintiff failed to make a showing of irreparable injury, as there was no threat to plaintiff being fully compensated, and, considering the public interest, there was significant potential harm to defendants, as they would have to bring multiple claims to collect refunded or uncollected mitigation fees. The court also held that there was little likelihood of plaintiff's success on the merits. Plaintiff's "Takings Clause" claim only involved regulatory restrictions on the use of property. The court thus applied a two prong test to determine that the regulation was reasonably related to the property and water control plans, and the fee reasonably served its purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff home owner's association's request for a preliminary injunction barring defendant, governmental water and resource agencies, from collecting mitigation fees as a condition for construction permits. Plaintiff failed to show that it would suffer irreparable harm or was likely to succeed on the merits on its Takings Clause claim. The fees were reasonably related to the purpose for which they were used.

Curran v. Mount Diablo Council of the Boy Scouts


No. B061869.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


48 Cal. App. 4th 670; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 580; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 281; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2208; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4118



March 29, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted June 2, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3108.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 23 Cal. App. 4th 1307 and 29 Cal. App. 4th 192 and 34 Cal. App. 4th 299 and 39 Cal. App. 4th 224 and 43 Cal. App. 4th 1370.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C365529, Sally Grant Disco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs of appeal are awarded to Mt. Diablo Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homosexual sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of defendant boy scouts council, in an action brought by plaintiff under the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51, based on defendant's rejection of plaintiff as a troop leader.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homosexual filed an action under the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51 after defendant boy scouts council refused to accept him as a troop leader. The trial court entered judgment in favor of defendant, and plaintiff appealed. Defendant cross-appealed. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, but reversed the trial court's decision that defendant was a business establishment under the Unruh Civil Rights Act. The court held that because of defendant's expressed belief that homosexuality was immoral, compelling defendant to accept a homosexual as a troop leader constituted an unlawful interference by the state in defendant's expressive association rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. In analyzing intimate association rights, the trial court should have focused on the local troop, rather than the larger council. The court found that the Unruh Civil Rights Act did not apply to the local troop because it was not a business establishment. Assuming that the trial court properly focused on the larger unit, the court found that defendant was also not a business establishment because it did not have a business purpose or offer traditional public accommodation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court to the extent that it found that the compelled hiring of plaintiff homosexual would constitute an unlawful interference by the state in the expressive association rights of defendant boy scouts council. However, the court reversed the trial court's decision that defendant was a business establishment within the meaning of the Unruh Civil Rights Act.

 

928 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hoogasian Flowers, Inc. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. C016105.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


23 Cal. App. 4th 1264; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 686; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 270; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2182; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4013



March 28, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I of the Discussion.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV530342, Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State Board of Equalization and intervenor San Francisco Educational Financing Authority sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff retailers, in an action brought by plaintiffs for refund of local sales taxes imposed by intervenor pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7286.1.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff retailers filed an action against defendant State Board of Equalization for refund of local sales tax imposed by intervenor San Francisco Educational Financial Authority pursuant to an ordinance approved by the majority but less than two-thirds of qualified electors. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs on the ground that the tax was subject to the two-third voter approval requirement of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 for special taxes imposed by special districts. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that intervenor was a special district within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 because it was created and controlled by the school district, which was an entity subject to the supermajority provision of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4. In addition, the court found that the tax in question was a special tax because it was levied to fund a specific government program; i.e., education and therefore, the tax was subject to Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4. The court held that because the tax was not approved by two-thirds of the qualified electors, it was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiff retailers because the local sales tax imposed by intervenor San Francisco Educational Financing Authority was invalid, as it was not approved by two-thirds of the qualified electors. The court found that intervenor was a special district and was therefore subject to the supermajority requirement of the state constitution.

Mendly v. County of Los Angeles


No. B073226.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


23 Cal. App. 4th 1193; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 822; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 261; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2160; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4034



March 25, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC017558, Kurt J. Lewin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed. Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a class of welfare recipients, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their motion to enforce judgment and for a permanent injunction in their suit against defendants, a county and its officials, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 17000.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a class of welfare recipients, entered a stipulated judgment with defendants, a county and its officials, in a suit which contested the level of benefits that defendants had set under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 17000. The judgment was to automatically expire if the legislature took control of obligations under § 17000, which the legislature did with Assembly Bill No. 2883, urgency legislation which abrogated such stipulated judgments. Plaintiffs filed a motion for enforcement of the stipulated judgment and for permanent injunctive relief. The trial court denied the motion and the appellate court affirmed. The court held that the stipulated judgment was not a contract subject to legislative impairment because it concerned only prospective payments which could not be put beyond the state's police power. The court further held that Assembly Bill No. 2883 did not violate the separation of powers clause of Cal. Const., art. III, § 3, because the stipulated judgment was not a final judgment that vested plaintiffs with rights which the legislature could not alter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied the motion of plaintiffs, a class of welfare recipients, for a permanent injunction and enforcement of a stipulated judgment because the benefits at issue were subject to the state's police power and plaintiffs' benefits were not vested under the judgment.

Powers v. City of Richmond


No. A056310.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


33 Cal. App. 4th 1347; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 814; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 251; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2061; 94 Daily Journal DAR 3839



March 23, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted June 30, 1994 (S039547).
Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 18 Cal. App. 4th 885, 23 Cal. App. 4th 648, 28 Cal. App. 4th 1672.) (See 10 Cal. 4th 85)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 9104616, David A. Dolgin, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens appealed from the denial of their petition for writ of mandate by the Contra Costa County Superior Court (California), in their action which sought to compel defendant city to provide plaintiffs with certain public agency financial records.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizens sought to compel defendant city to provide plaintiffs with certain public agency financial records. After both the lower court and the court denied plaintiffs' petitions for writ of mandate, and the supreme court denied review, plaintiffs filed a notice of appeal with the court, to which defendant responded by filing a motion to dismiss. The court granted defendant's motion and dismissed the appeal because Cal. Gov't Code § 6259(c) limited review of orders pertaining to public records requests to a petition to the appellate court for issuance of an extraordinary writ, which the court had previously denied. The court rejected plaintiffs' contention that § 6259(c) violated their constitutional rights because "appellate jurisdiction," as provided in Cal. Const. art. VI, § 11, did not confer a right to "appeal." Similarly, it was not unconstitutional for the legislature to limit review to an extraordinary writ as it did pursuant to § 6259(c). Therefore, plaintiffs exhausted their only avenue of review when the court previously denied their writ petition and the supreme court denied review.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant city's motion to dismiss plaintiff citizens' appeal, because plaintiffs did not have a constitutional right to appeal after their petition for extraordinary writ was denied, in their action to compel defendant to provide them with certain public agency financial records.

Independent Roofing Contractors v. Department of Industrial Relations


No. A059714.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


23 Cal. App. 4th 345; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 550; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 236; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1955; 1 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 1577; 94 Daily Journal DAR 3608; 128 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P57,691



March 17, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 943633, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondents shall be awarded their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant building contractor associations challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandate regarding respondent Department of Industrial Relations' decision to delete a particular wage determination for public construction contracts.


OVERVIEW: Appellant building contractor associations filed a petition for writ of mandate against respondent Department of Industrial Relations that asserted constitutional challenges to respondent's decision to delete a particular wage determination. The trial court denied the petition, and the court affirmed on appeal. The court determined that appellants had a sufficient interest in the determination to give them standing to proceed. However, the court held that, absent a showing of failure to exercise discretion, respondent's decision to rely on the existence of a collective bargaining agreement as the basis for the wage determination did not establish an unconstitutional delegation of its legislative authority to private parties. The court ruled that respondent's action was not arbitrary and capricious, as no statute prevented respondent from determining that the information provided was insufficient to support a wage determination. The court determined that because respondent exercised delegated quasi-legislative power, there was no constitutional requirement to hold hearings or give notice before making its wage determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant building contractor associations' petition for writ of mandate because respondent Department of Industrial Relations' decision to delete a wage determination did not constitute an unconstitutional delegation of rulemaking power and was not arbitrary or capricious.

Korean American Legal Advocacy Foundation v. City of Los Angeles


No. B077272. , No. B079224.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


23 Cal. App. 4th 376; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 530; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 238; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1962; 94 Daily Journal DAR 3558



March 17, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing April 15, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 331. Review Denied July 14, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3928.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC080635, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal (B077272) is dismissed as moot. The petition for writ of mandate (B079224) is denied. The temporary stay is dissolved and the matter remanded for further proceedings. Each party to bear its own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs retailers sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their request for a preliminary injunction and sustained defendant city's demurrer without leave to amend as to their cause of action alleging that Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22 and the Alcohol Beverage Control Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23000 et seq., preempted an ordinance governing conditional uses.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs retailers brought an action to prevent defendant city from imposing conditions on the rebuilding of destroyed or damaged businesses, and on the operation of existing businesses under threat of revoking their deemed approved status under a city ordinance governing conditional uses. The trial court denied plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injunction and sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend. The court affirmed the order of the trial court, as Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22 and the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23000 et seq., did not preempt defendant from exercising its zoning and land use authority over businesses that were licensed to sell alcohol for off-site consumption. The ordinance did not regulate alcohol sales, but were instead designed to abate nuisances in the vicinity of these businesses. The court held that the new ordinance applied to plaintiffs' businesses because they were no longer "grandfathered" or "deemed approved" conditional uses, due to a break in continuous operation. The court held that plaintiffs' appeal from the denial of the preliminary injunction was moot because the underlying claim had been dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiffs retailers' claim alleging state preemption of a local zoning and land use ordinance because the ordinance did not regulate or control alcohol sales. The court also dismissed plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injunction for mootness.

 

933 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



American Federation of Labor v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


No. B068526.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX.


23 Cal. App. 4th 51; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 210; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 209; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1781; 9 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 490; 94 Daily Journal DAR 3164



March 9, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Deneal of Rehearing April 5, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 296.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 114425, Edwin M. Osborne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court upholding the validity of In the Matter of David Hayes, supra, Cal. Unemp. Ins. App. Bd. Precedent Benefit Dec. No. P-B-470, is affirmed. Appellant's request for attorney fees pursuant to Code of Civil Procedure section 1021.5 is denied. Costs to respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff labor organizations appealed from the declaratory ruling of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which concluded that defendant board could find an employee ineligible for unemployment insurance based on dismissal from employment for refusing to take a drug test.


OVERVIEW: A worker on an offshore oil drilling platform was discharged for refusing to submit to a urinalysis drug test. He was therefore denied unemployment benefits based on misconduct under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code, § 1256. In a declaratory judgment action by plaintiff labor organizations, the trial court concluded it was permissible for defendant board to deny unemployment benefits based on misconduct. On appeal, the court affirmed. Even though there was no cause to believe the employee was under the influence of illegal drugs on the job, he was employed in a hazardous work environment. The employer had a reasonable interest in having its employees submit to annual and periodic drug screens. The employee himself testified that his work environment was hazardous. Given the hazards to all employees working on the oil-drilling platform who could injure themselves, if impaired, and jeopardize the safety of others who might have to come to their rescue, the drug testing requirement was reasonable. Even though the employee's specific job might not be considered safety sensitive in another location, the hazardous environment in which he worked, by his own admission, made it so in this case.


OUTCOME: In the action for declaratory ruling by plaintiff labor organizations, the court concluded that defendant board could deny an employee unemployment compensation benefits for misconduct in refusing to take a drug test, which resulted in his discharge from work.

Suman v. BMW of North America, Inc.


No. B061162.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


23 Cal. App. 4th 1; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 133; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 199; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1760; 94 Daily Journal DAR 3108



March 8, 1994, Decided  
March 8, 1994


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied June 2, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3114.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC51549, Robert M. Letteau, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is reversed in part and affirmed in part. Insofar as the judgment awarded plaintiff zero dollars in civil penalties according to the jury's special verdict, the judgment is reversed. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. The cause is remanded for further trial on the issue of civil penalties. Costs on appeal to plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) awarding zero dollars in civil penalties for a car that he alleged was defectively manufactured, after construing certain civil penalty provisions of the Song-Beverly Consumer Act in favor of defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued defendant for manufacturing an allegedly defective car. Defendant failed to return the consideration paid by plaintiff for the car or to replace the vehicle. The trial court construed certain civil penalty provisions of the Song-Beverly Consumer Warranty Act in favor of defendant. The court reversed the zero dollar award in civil penalties. When the trial court concluded that Cal. Civ. Code § 1794 (c) requirement of willfulness applied also to § 1794 (e), and when it, in effect, instructed the jury that § 1794 (c)-type willfulness was the sole basis for awarding civil penalties, the court ignored a special distinction made by the legislature with respect to the seller of new automobiles. In doing so, the court erred. The error was prejudicial because it prevented the jurors from considering the specific penalty provisions in § 1794 (e) in making a potential award.


OUTCOME: The judgment awarding plaintiff zero dollars in civil penalties was reversed because the court improperly construed specific civil penalty provisions of the Song-Beverly consumer Warranty Act in favor of defendant. The error was prejudicial.

 

935 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Clark)


No. G014522.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


22 Cal. App. 4th 1541; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 46; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 180; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1594; 94 Daily Journal DAR 2708



February 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-95992, Jean N. Rheinheimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the superior court to vacate its order granting defendants' motion to dismiss the special circumstance allegations under Penal Code section 995, and to enter a new and different order denying the motion. All stays previously issued by this court in this case are lifted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel the Superior Court of Orange County (California) to reverse its order dismissing the murder charges against defendants in accordance with Cal. Penal Code § 995.


OVERVIEW: Defendants robbed a store, and in the process killed an innocent woman. Defendants were charged with murder and moved to dismiss pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995. Defendants contended the status of the law was uncertain at the time the murder was committed and that a reasonable person would not have known whether their actions were subject to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2, amended by proposition 114 or by proposition 115. The trial court dismissed the charges and the state petitioned for a writ of mandate reviewing the trial court's judgment. The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate, vacated the trial court's judgment dismissing the charges, and ordered preparation of a new and different order denying defendants' motion to dismiss. The court held no conflict existed between the aforementioned propositions and proposition 115 was properly applied to defendants and was effective at the time they committed the crime. Additionally, the court held that given the court's recent history with respect to proposition 115, defendants had sufficient knowledge of the potential penalty assessed by violating the statute; thus, defendants were not deprived of their due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate ordering the trial court to vacate its prior order dismissing defendants' murder charges, and ordering the trial court to issue a new order denying defendants' motions to dismiss. The court held that defendants had proper notice of the potential penalty relating to the above-mentioned offense, both statutes were in effect at the time defendants committed the crime and no conflict existed.

In re York


No. H011030, No. H011177, No. H011242.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


33 Cal. App. 4th 712; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 771; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 146; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1376; 93 Daily Journal DAR 2250



February 22, 1994, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 26, 1994 (S031285).
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 22 Cal. App. 4th 1217 and 28 Cal. App. 4th 31.)] (See 9 Cal. 4th 1133)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 161088, 161238, 161957, 163882, and 163935, John J. Garibaldi and Robert Michael Foley, Judges; Municipal Court for the Santa Clara Judicial District of Santa Clara County, Nos. C9327030, C9327239, C9327432, C9327583, C9327609 and C9327981, Marliese G. Kim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The writs of habeas corpus are granted. For any petitioner whose underlying criminal prosecution has not yet concluded, and who is still subject to conditions of OR release, the court shall vacate those portions of his or her supervised OR release order which require submission to warrantless drug testing and search and seizure. If the prosecutor elects within 30 days to request additional conditions of OR release for any such petitioner, the court shall consider that request in conformity with the opinions [***2] expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner arrestees filed writs of habeas corpus seeking review of the decision from the Superior Court, Santa Clara County (California) which held that petitioners subject to supervised own recognizance release were to be subject to the condition that they submit to drug testing and warrantless search and seizure.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner arrestees were all subject to conditions of own recognizance release that were summarily imposed instead of determined on a case by case basis. Petitioners challenged the conditions, which included drug testing and warrantless search and seizure. The court granted petitioners' writs of habeas corpus and noted that the legislature intended to give the magistrate or court broad discretion to impose any reasonable conditions of own recognizance release upon defendants. The court also noted that this broad discretion precluded petitioners' arguments that no conditions could be imposed that were irrelevant to petitioners' future appearance in court. The court held that restrictions, while allowed, in order not to unreasonably restrain the liberties which petitioners retained under both federal and state constitutions, were to be determined on a case by case basis. The court therefore granted the writs as to those petitioners who were required to submit to drug testing and warrantless search and seizure, but gave prosecutors 30 days to request additional conditions for individual petitioners.


OUTCOME: Petitioners' writs of habeas corpus were granted because magistrates could not impose conditions on a subject of own recognizance release without first making a determination on a case by case basis.

Healing v. California Coastal Com.


No. B074129.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


22 Cal. App. 4th 1158; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 758; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 148; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1348; 93 Daily Journal DAR 2265



February 22, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC055465, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed in its entirety, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of administrative mandate directing the Coastal Commission to refer Healing's permit application to the ERB for the ERB's prompt review and recommendations or, if there is still no ERB in existence, to disregard the County's proposed LCP on the ground the County has abandoned any intent it might once have had to become a permitting authority. After the Commission responds to the writ, the trial court shall take such further action as necessary to carry out the views expressed in this opinion. Healing is awarded his costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that there was no unconstitutional taking that occurred in conjunction with respondent, the California Coastal Commission, denying appellant's application for a coastal development permit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant submitted an application to respondent, California Coastal Commission, for a coastal development permit in order to build a home. The application was denied for a variety of reasons, including that the Commission could not be sure whether another agency, that apparently did not even exist, might conclude that appellant's house would have a significant adverse effect on the area. Appellant sought judicial review by filing a petition for a writ of administrative mandate and complaint for inverse condemnation. At the hearing, the trial court denied appellant's motion for summary adjudication and found there was no unconstitutional taking. On review, the court held that the takings issues raised by appellant's inverse condemnation action alleging a regulatory taking arising from the Coastal Commission's denial of his development permit were to be determined in a court trial. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed and the matter remanded with instructions to the Coastal Commission to process appellant's permit application consistent with the court's opinion, and for the trial court to take such further action as necessary to carry out the court's views.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the matter remanded with instructions to the Coastal Commission to process appellant's permit application consistent with the court's opinion, and for the trial court to take such further action as necessary to carry out the court's views in light of the court's finding that a trial was necessary to adjudicate the constitutional taking issues if no permit was issued.

 

938 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Kuykendall v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. D019620. , No. D020023.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


22 Cal. App. 4th 1194; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 783; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 153; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1364; 93 Daily Journal DAR 2342



February 22, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied May 12, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2521.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 660701, Lawrence Kapiloff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and orders are reversed. The petition for mandate is dismissed as moot. The Board shall have costs on appeal from Kuykendall.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State Board of Equalization and the State of California, appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which ordered defendants to create a refund plan for unconstitutionally collected sales taxes in plaintiff taxpayers' class action suit. The trial court found that the legislative refund plan contained in Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7275 et seq., did not apply to the pending litigation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers commenced a class action against defendants, the State Board of Equalization and the State of California, seeking a refund of unconstitutionally collected sales taxes that were to support the San Diego County Regional Justice Facility. The trial court ordered defendants to submit a plan to refund the taxes but, during the pendency of the litigation, the legislature enacted Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7275 et seq., which, by its terms, constituted the sole manner of obtaining refunds. Defendants appealed and the court reversed the trial court's order, dismissing defendants' petition for mandate as moot. The court found that the trial court's judgment created no vested rights and that the legislature clearly intended the statutory scheme to apply to the pending litigation. The court further found that Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 32, gave the legislature plenary power to create the refund scheme through statute and that the statute was not unconstitutional, although it created different classes of taxpayers and although its ends might have been achieved through different means.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment awarded in favor of plaintiff taxpayers in their class action suit against defendants, the state board of equalization and the state, and found that the legislative scheme for obtaining sales tax refunds was constitutional and was the sole refund method available to plaintiffs. The court then dismissed as moot defendants' petition for mandate.

People v. Sangani


No. A059233.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


22 Cal. App. 4th 1120; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 158; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 139; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1273; 94 Daily Journal DAR 2181



February 18, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 16, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 234.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. C105020, David C. Lee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants metal plating company and its owner appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), convicting defendant of misdemeanor violations of the hazardous waste control act, and felony violations being Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25189(a) and (b), placing defendants on five years' probation and imposing a total of $ 400,000 in fines.


OVERVIEW: Defendants metal plating company and its owner appealed their conviction for violations of the California Hazardous Waste Control Act, Cal. Health & Safety. Code § 25100 et seq., and Cal. Code Regs. 22, § 66001 et seq. The court affirmed, finding that the convictions were proper where California had not adopted an unrestricted domestic sewage exemption and the publicly owned treatment works was not a permitted facility for accepting hazardous waste through sewage lines. The court found that the laboratory results were admissible because the relevant statutes did not specifically provide that evidence should be excluded for failure to use a certified laboratory. The court found that defendants did not overcome the presumption that the court used the correct standard for intent. The court found sufficient evidence of all convictions. The court also found that defendants' vagueness challenge to their felony convictions was frivolous in light of the rule that one to whose conduct a statute clearly applied could not successfully challenge it for vagueness and that the relevant statutes and regulations were not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendants' conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendants metal plating company and its owner of misdemeanor and felony violations of the California Hazardous Waste Control Act because it was illegal to dump the hazardous waste at the facility in which defendants dumped, the evidence was sufficient to support the convictions and the statutes were not unconstitutionally vague.

Askew v. Askew

February 17, 1994, Decided

DISPOSITION: The trial court erred in not dismissing Ronald's complaint, most of which was substantively for breach of promise and any arguable remainder was beyond the jurisdiction of the civil court in light of the ongoing family law action. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) finding that she had committed fraud on her husband in connection with their marriage and imposing a constructive trust on appellant's interest in joint property. Appellant sought review of that decision and the denial of her motion to tax certain costs.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought divorce from respondent. Respondent filed a separate civil lawsuit alleging fraud. Respondent's complaint alleged that appellant had never loved him but had represented to him that she did in order to induce him to marry her and transfer property. The jury found that appellant had indeed made a false representation as to a past or existing material fact, and awarded damages suffered by respondent in reliance on the truth of that representation. The jury next found that appellant owed a duty of trust and confidence to respondent to hold the property for the plaintiff and his children. They determined she breached that duty by failing to convey the property upon request. The court entered judgment in accordance with the jury findings and denied appellant's motion to tax certain costs. The court concluded that the trial court erred in not dismissing respondent's complaint, most of which was substantively for breach of promise and precluded by the California anti-heart-balm statute, Cal. Civ. Code § 43.5. Any arguable claims left to be determined was beyond the jurisdiction of the civil court in light of the ongoing family law action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment with directions to enter judgment in favor of appellant wife reversed the order denying expert witness fees, and ordered respondent husband to pay the attorney fees of appellant.

Irwin v. City of Hemet


No. E010679. , No. E010851.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


22 Cal. App. 4th 507; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 433; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 105; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1080; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1808



February 10, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts A and B.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 204821, Charles D. Field, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In summary, the motion for summary judgment should have been denied as to all defendants on the wrongful death claim, and should have been denied as to the City of Hemet on the section 1983 claim. While the motion was properly granted as to the individual defendants on the section 1983 claim, the one-final-judgment rule precludes the entry of any judgment until all claims against those defendants have been resolved. Accordingly, the judgment is reversed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs survivors of deceased prisoner appealed the grant of summary judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), in favor of defendants, city and individual police officers, in an action brought by plaintiffs for damages for wrongful death and the deprivation of civil rights pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs survivors of deceased prisoner filed a complaint against defendants, city and individual police officers, which alleged damages pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and wrongful death as a result of a prisoner, who was plaintiff's relative, committing suicide in defendants' jail by hanging himself. Defendants moved for summary judgment, which the trial court granted to all defendants on the wrongful death count. At a subsequent hearing, after further discovery, summary judgment was granted to all defendants on the § 1983 claim. Plaintiffs appealed. The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to defendants and held that summary judgment should have been denied to all defendants on the wrongful death claim and should have been denied as to defendant city on the § 1983 claim because plaintiffs failed to show that the individual defendants knew or should have known that the prisoner was likely to kill himself, and defendant city failed to establish as a matter of undisputed fact any lack of liability on their part.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of defendants, city and individual police officers, was reversed because it should have been denied to all defendants on the wrongful death claim and to defendant city on the civil rights claim as plaintiffs survivors of deceased prisoner failed to show that the individual defendants knew or should have known that the prisoner was likely to kill himself, and defendant city failed to establish a lack of liability.
