Willis v. State of California


No. C015594.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


22 Cal. App. 4th 287; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 413; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 97; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1019; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1669



February 8, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV521742, Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As a last gasp on appeal, plaintiff alleges that section 12479 is unconstitutional because it discriminates against an identifiable group solely based on marital status and thereby constitutes a denial of equal protection. We hesitate to decipher plaintiff's raw accusation, lacking as it does citation to legal authority or to the record. We accept the Attorney General's generic response that it is incumbent upon plaintiff to demonstrate more than differential treatment. The Legislature controls the disposition of property ( Kizer v. Hanna (1989) 48 Cal.3d 1, 10 [255 Cal.Rptr. 412, 767 P.2d 679]), and may treat one class of persons differently from another provided there is a rational connection to a legitimate state objective. ( Hale v. Morgan (1978) 22 Cal.3d 388, 395 [149 Cal.Rptr. 375, 584 P.2d 512]; Peters v. Superior Court (1989) 212 Cal.App.3d 218, 226 [260 Cal.Rptr. 426]; Baker v. City of Santa Monica (1986) 181 Cal.App.3d 972, 983 [226 Cal.Rptr. 755].) [***2] Plaintiff has failed to demonstrate a plausible deprivation of equal protection as assured under both the state and federal Constitutions. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the widow of a state employee who had pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 12479 designated his daughter as the recipient of all state warrants due him at his death, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of respondent state on appellant's claim that her interest in the payments as community property under Cal. Civ. Code § 5250 trumped the daughter's interest.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Gov't Code § 12479 mandated respondent state to pay all state warrants due an employee at his death to his designee "notwithstanding any other provision of law." Appellant was the widow of a state employee who had designated his daughter by an earlier marriage as his section § 12479 designee. The trial court granted respondent summary judgment on appellant's claim that respondent was required to withhold payment from the deceased's designee, notwithstanding § 12479, because of the widow's interest in the payments as community property under Cal. Civ. Code § 5250. On appeal, appellant argued that § 5250's later enacted and specific provisions governing the disposition of community property impliedly repealed the broad language of Cal. Gov't Code § 12479. The court affirmed. The court held that because the § 12479 language was unmistakably plain, it could not be repealed by implication unless a later enacted statute gave unmistakable evidence of an intent to repeal its language -- and § 5250 did not. The court also rejected appellant's equal protection claim because any differential treatment was rationally related to a legitimate governmental purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment awarding summary judgment to respondent state because appellant widow could not show unmistakable evidence that a later enacted community property statute impliedly repealed the broad language of a statute which mandated respondent state to pay all warrants due a deceased employee at his death to a designee other than the widow.

Evans v. City of Bakersfield


No. F016745.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


22 Cal. App. 4th 321; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 406; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 114; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1137; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1941



February 8, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 208234, Roger D. Randall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Costs are awarded to appellant City.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city and police officer, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which entered a verdict for plaintiff detainee in his action for state tort causes of action and federal civil rights violations, arising out of defendants' detention of plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff detainee's neck was fractured in an altercation with defendant police officer, who was either conducting a patdown search of plaintiff or attempting to handcuff him. Plaintiff brought an action for damages against defendants, city and police officer, alleging state and federal causes of action. The jury found by general verdict, and assessed damages, in favor of plaintiff. Defendants sought review of the decision. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the trial court committed error in instructing the jury that plaintiff had a right to use reasonable force to resist an unlawful detention, because no such right existed. The court found the error prejudicial, because it allowed the jury to overlook as irrelevant plaintiff's actions after the initial detention and whether they set in motion the chain of events which followed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment awarding damages to plaintiff detainee in his action against defendants, city and police officer, because it was prejudicial error to instruct the jury that plaintiff had a right to use reasonable force to resist an unlawful detention.

Tobe v. City of Santa Ana


No. G014257, No. G014536.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


32 Cal. App. 4th 941; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 386; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 86; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 889; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1449



February 2, 1994, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 12, 1994 (S038530), Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2494.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 22 Cal. App. 4th 228 and 27 Cal. App. 4th 1209.)] (See 9 Cal. 4th 1069)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 696000, James L. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court denying the petition for writ of mandate in part is reversed with directions to issue the writ as prayed. Appellants shall have their costs. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the municipal court to sustain the demurrers to the counts pleading violations of the Santa Ana camping ordinance.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner consolidated defendants sought a writ of mandate against respondent City of Santa Ana (California), which charged petitioners, who were homeless, with violating Santa Ana Mun. Code, ch. 10, art. VIII, § 10-400 et seq., or "the camping ordinance". Petitioners challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, consolidated defendants, were a group of homeless individuals who had been prosecuted for violating the camping ordinance of Santa Ana Mun. Code, ch. 10, art. VIII, § 10-400 et seq., which made it an offense to "camp" or "store" personal property in public in the City of Santa Ana. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate against enforcement of the ordinance and challenged its constitutionality. The court issued the writ of mandate, found the ordinance unconstitutional, and ordered the municipal court for respondent City of Santa Ana to sustain petitioner's demurrers to the violations of the ordinance. The court found that the ordinance punished the homeless specifically and as such was cruel and unusual punishment of an involuntary status. The court held that, if allowed to stand, the ordinance would turn the jail into a poorhouse and would thus destroy liberty. The court further found the ordinance was vague and overbroad and found that the definitions within the ordinance made no distinction between camping and picnicking or between camping paraphernalia and a student's backpack and that this offended the due process clauses of both the state and federal Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate and ordered respondent City of Santa Ana to sustain petitioners consolidated defendant's demurrers to the violations of the camping ordinance because the ordinance was cruel and unusual punishment and was vague and overbroad.

Overton v. Superior Court


No. G014877.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


22 Cal. App. 4th 112; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 274; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 76; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 823; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1304



January 31, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 8, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-87988, David O. Carter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue compelling Judge David O. Carter to accept Overton's challenge and recuse himself from all further proceedings in People of the State of California, Plaintiff, v. Richard K. Overton, Defendant (Super. Ct. Orange County, No. C-87988). The alternative writ is discharged and the stay is dissolved. To avoid further delays, this opinion will be final 15 days from the date it is filed. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 24(d).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner client appealed the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied petitioner's motion to have the judge disqualify himself, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6(2).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner client applied for a writ of mandate which would compel the trial judge to disqualify himself from further proceedings in the pending murder case. The lower court denied petitioner's motion to have the judge disqualify himself, and ruled in favor of respondent court. The court had previously declared a mistrial and found the need to relieve original counsel, due to a conflict involving accusations of evidence tampering. Petitioner's new counsel filed a preemptory challenge to the trial judge, which the trial judge found to be untimely and overruled. The court had ordered the trial judge to declare the mistrial after an extraordinary delay and efforts by the trial judge to save the case. Upon review, the court granted a preemptory writ of mandate in favor of petitioner, holding that disqualification of the trial judge was mandatory, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6(2), as the petitioner had been granted a new trial as part of his writ relief.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's writ of mandate compelling the trial judge to disqualify himself from further proceedings in the pending murder case, finding that disqualification was mandatory because petitioner was entitled to timely demand a new judge if a new trial was granted as part of writ relief.

Drake v. Superior Court


No. D019658.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


21 Cal. App. 4th 1826; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 829; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 63; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 646; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1077



January 27, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. D363088 and D369621, Alan B. Clements, Commissioner, and Wesley R. Mason III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged, through writ of mandate, the decision of respondent, the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which refused to let him act as an attorney at law, in appellant's actions under his power of attorney, Cal. Civ. Code § 2494.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner agent sought to act as an attorney at law for two individuals in their family law actions. Petitioner cited the Power of Attorney Act, Cal. Civ. Code 2494 as his authority. Respondent trial court refused to let him bring the actions. Petitioner sought review through writ of mandate, contending that he had a valid power of attorney and was just standing in for individuals bringing pro se actions. On appeal the court denied the writ, holding that petitioner's construction of the Power of Attorney Act would result in absurdity as it was inconsistent with laws prohibiting the unauthorized practice of law and it would sanction criminal conduct. Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6125, 6126. The court ruled that the authority of attorneys in fact under § 2494 was subject to California State Bar Act's prohibition against the practice of law by nonlawyers, and that none of the enumerated powers in section § 2494 expressly allows attorneys in fact to practice law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of mandate to petitioner agent because if the power of attorney act was construed to allow him to stand in for pro se litigants, it would result in absurdity as it would be inconsistent with laws prohibiting the unauthorized practice of law and it would sanction criminal conduct, and nothing in the act authorized him to act at law.

Evans v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Nos. C011373, C012371.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


21 Cal. App. 4th 958; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 460; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 12; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 289; 94 Daily Journal DAR 552



January 12, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV364057, Cecily Bond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's judgment granting Evans's petition for writ of mandate and its subsequent order awarding Evans attorney fees are reversed. The trial court is directed to issue a new order denying Evans's petition and request for attorney fees. Evans shall pay DMV's costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, department of motor vehicles and its director, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), granting writ of mandate to respondent automobile dismantler on the ground that the administrative hearing leading to the decision to revoke his business license violated his right to due process of law because he did not receive actual notice of the hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, department of motor vehicles and its director, issued an accusation against respondent automobile dismantler, seeking to suspend his license. The accusation and a notice of hearing was sent to respondent at his established place of business by certified mail and it was signed for by a tenant at the address. Respondent did not appear at the hearing and the administrative law judge (ALJ) revoked his license. Respondent's request for reconsideration was granted based on his contention that he did not receive the notice. The ALJ's original decision was ultimately adopted by appellants. The trial court granted respondent's petition for writ of mandate and held that service was not effected and the hearing was therefore unconstitutional. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the service complied with Cal. Gov't Code § 11505 and § 8311 and satisfied due process of law because the notice procedures were reasonably certain to accomplish actual notice. The court held that appellants had discretion to grant relief to respondent for his failure to attend the hearing, but its refusal to provide a de novo hearing on the substantive issues was not an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of a petition for writ of mandate and award of attorney fees to respondent automobile dismantler because service of a notice of a disciplinary hearing satisfied due process. The court directed the trial court to issue a new order denying respondent's petition and request for attorney fees and ordering respondent to pay the costs on appeal of appellants, department of motor vehicles and its director.

 

948 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Troost Monument Co. v. City of Santa Monica


No. B066850.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


22 Cal. App. 4th 246; 27 Cal. Rptr. 2d 240; 1994 Cal. App. LEXIS 90; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 918; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1534



January 6, 1994, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Ordered Published February 4, 1994. Review Denied March 24, 1994 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC141904, Hiroshi Fujisaki, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Each party is to bear its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found that a city ordinance regarding cemeteries was invalid in a suit filed by appellee company.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city argued that a decision that found that a city ordinance permitting the sale of markers at a city-owned cemetery was invalid in a suit filed by appellee company was improper because the ordinance was not preempted by a state statute. The reviewing court upheld the ruling. Cal. Health & Safety Code § 8137 prohibited a city-owned cemetery from selling markers, and the reviewing court found that it applied to charter cities, such as appellant. Because Cal. Health & Safety Code § 8137 was part of a comprehensive series of provisions devoted to the statewide concern with the disposition of remains, the ordinance was not a purely municipal affair, so the state statute did not violate the home rule provisions of Cal. Const., art. XI, § 5. Accordingly, the ordinance was invalid and preempted by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 8137.


OUTCOME: A decision that found that found that appellant city's ordinance regarding cemeteries was invalid in a suit filed by appellee company was affirmed because it was contrary to a statutory scheme devoted to the disposition of remains.

Sierra Club v. Department of Forestry & Fire Protection


Nos. A056250, A058664


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


21 Cal. App. 4th 603; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 338; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1336; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 89; 94 Daily Journal DAR 129



December 29, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 18, 1994 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1388.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Humboldt County, Nos. 82983 and 83428, William F. Ferroggiaro, Jr., and Roy G. MacFarland, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The trial court judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Real party in interest lumber company sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California), which invalidated timber harvest plans approved by defendant forestry department without mitigation measures proposed by fish and game department to protect rare or endangered species.


OVERVIEW: Defendant forestry department approved two timber harvest plans (THP's) submitted by real party in interest lumber company without mitigation measures proposed by the fish and game department to reduce the effect of logging on wildlife including rare animal species which were dependent on old-growth forests for survival. The rare or endangered marbled murrelet had been detected on both sites and used these sites for nesting. The trial court invalidated the THP's and real party in interest sought review. The court consolidated the cases for review and affirmed finding that defendant's decision to approve these THP's was a prejudicial abuse of discretion, and was not supported by applicable law or substantial evidence in the record showing adequate consideration of mitigation measures to protect certain rare animal species shown to be dependent on old-growth forests. The court also found that the claim that the state would effect an unconstitutional taking of private property by protecting those species was not ripe for review because the state regulations would be applied to the private property in the future and the effect on the property was uncertain.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial courts' invalidation of the timber harvest plans because defendant forestry department's approval of those plans was an abuse of discretion not supported by law or substantial evidence showing adequate consideration of measures to protect rare species.

Savage v. Pacific Gas & Electric Co.


No. A057595.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


21 Cal. App. 4th 434; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 305; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1318; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9753; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16637; 22 Media L. Rep. 1737



December 27, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 17, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1355.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 889060, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it dismisses the second cause of action for intentional interference with prospective economic advantage, the fourth cause of action for interference with contractual relations, the seventh cause of action for slander, and the eighth cause of action for libel. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. Costs to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff journalist appealed from the judgment on the pleadings and summary adjudication that was entered by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) in favor of defendant utility in an action alleging, inter alia, libel, slander, and interference with employment contract, economic relationships, and ratepayers' rights, where plaintiff was fired after defendant's officers accused her of a conflict-of-interest.


OVERVIEW: Prior to being hired by an energy trade publication, plaintiff journalist ended her membership with a group that opposed nuclear power. Later, officers of defendant utility contacted her employer and accused her of having a conflict-of-interest, alleging that a member of the activist group had said that plaintiff was still doing work for them. Consequently, plaintiff was fired. She similarly lost a free-lance position with another publication after an officer contacted it and accused her of bias. Plaintiff sued for libel, slander, and interference with contract, economic relationships, and ratepayers' rights, but judgment on the pleadings and summary adjudication was entered for defendant. Plaintiff appealed. In affirming in part, the court ruled that the ratepayers' rights claim was unsupported by the evidence. In reversing in part with respect to the libel, slander, and the other interference claims, the court ruled, inter alia, that blacklisting plaintiff served no legitimate purpose under U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 453(a). Also, factual disputes existed as to certain defamatory comments, the issue of intentional conduct, and privilege to interfere.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed as to the interference with ratepayers' rights claim because the claim was unsupported by the evidence. The judgment was reversed in part with respect to the libel, slander, and the other interference claims because, inter alia, certain comments were not protected by the First Amendment and issues of fact existed regarding certain defamatory comments, the issue of intentional conduct, and privilege to interfere.

Berman v. City of Daly City

COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR

The trial court granted summary judgment to defendant city and defendant police officer. The trial court held that defendants were immune from liability under Cal. Veh. On appeal, the court reversed the grant of summary judgment to defendant city on plaintiff's state cause of action, but affirmed the trial court's judgment in all other respects. Defendant city's vehicular pursuit policy did not provide the minimum guidelines required to qualify for immunity under §17004.7(c)(4).

People v. Morse


Nos. A058935, A060033.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


21 Cal. App. 4th 259; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 816; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1299; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9629; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16528



December 22, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 17, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1356.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. H-156662-0 and H-156662-0A, Mark L. Eaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal from the order granting the preliminary injunction is dismissed as moot. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which enjoined him under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17537.6 from mailing solicitations to the public concerning homestead declarations; the court also issued civil penalties against appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney argued that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17537.6, regulating solicitations, was an unconstitutional restraint of his right to free speech under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I. Appellant asserted that the letters he sent to the public to hire him to assist in filing homestead declarations were protected commercial speech. Because appellant's solicitations did not explain that there was a difference between a homestead exemption and a declared homestead, the court held that the solicitations were deceptive and misleading and they were not entitled to commercial speech protection. The court rejected appellant's arguments that the statute was unconstitutionally vague as applied to lawyers because its legislative history clearly indicated that its purpose was to prevent letters such as those that appellant had been sending to the public. Accordingly, the court affirmed the issuance of the injunction against appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the injunction and penalties issued against appellant attorney because the solicitations that he sent to the public were deceptive and misleading and were not protected commercial speech.

People v. Lewis


No. A058378.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


21 Cal. App. 4th 243; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 827; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1300; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9625; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16521



December 22, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 24, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. SC27968, Clarence B. Knight, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which sentenced appellant to a total prison term of 16 years for voluntary manslaughter, which included a consecutive three-year term for a great bodily injury enhancement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of attempted voluntary manslaughter and brandishing a deadly weapon. The trial court sentenced appellant to a total state prison term of 16 years. Appellant contended that the consecutive three-year term for the great bodily injury enhancement must be stricken as exempt from Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7. The court affirmed the sentence and held that in the face of the clear language of § 12022.7, there was no reason to construe that it exempted attempted manslaughter from the enhancement for infliction of great bodily injury. The court also ruled that under prior analogous authority and legislative history, there was no reason to construe § 12022.7 to exempt attempted manslaughter. The court held that the enhanced sentencing range for attempted manslaughter was not disproportionate to the sentencing range for assault with a deadly weapon, similarly enhanced.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed sentencing appellant to a consecutive three-year term for a great bodily injury enhancement, because there was no reason to construe that the enhancement statute exempted attempted manslaughter from the enhancement for infliction of great bodily injury.

Ramona Convent of the Holy Names v. City of Alhambra

COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE

* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) that granted summary judgment to appellee city in appellant's action seeking, inter alia, damages for inverse condemnation and violation of civil rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city's zoning plan changed the designation for appellant's property from multiple family to open space. Appellant subsequently filed an application for a tentative tract map providing for a lot split to divide the property. Appellee's planning commission and city council denied appellant's application. Appellant filed suit, seeking, inter alia, damages for inverse condemnation and violation of civil rights. The trial court granted summary judgment to appellee, holding that appellant's inverse condemnation claim was not ripe and its civil rights claim was barred on grounds of immunity. Moreover, appellee did not violate appellant's right to free exercise of religion.

Brown v. State of California


No. G012337.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


21 Cal. App. 4th 1500; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 687; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1342; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 419; 94 Daily Journal DAR 683



December 17, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published January 18, 1994. Review Denied March 17, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1378.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 639574, David H. Brickner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Respondent shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which dismissed his complaint against the state for inverse condemnation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 19, after sustaining the state's demurrer without leave to amend. The state contended that the action was untimely and the property was taken under valid police power.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was one of several others who held trust deeds on property that the state took in order to undertake hazardous waste cleanup with the federal government under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25301 et seq., and the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation and Liability Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 9601 et seq. Appellant brought an action against the state for inverse condemnation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. The state filed a demurrer claiming that the action was untimely and failed to state a cause of action because the property was taken under the valid police power. The trial court sustained the demurrer and dismissed appellant's complaint. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment, and held that the taking was not for a use that was public in character requiring compensation, but was to accomplish hazardous waste cleanup for the health and safety of the surrounding residents and promote the general welfare. The court also held the action was barred by the statute of limitations, and appellant's complaint stated on its face that the property had been taken 10 years previously.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which dismissed appellant's complaint against the state for inverse condemnation because the property was not taken for a public purpose, but was taken to clean up hazardous wastes which posed a threat to the safety and health of surrounding residents, and because the action was untimely under that statute of limitations.

DeVita v. County of Napa


No. A059429.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


31 Cal. App. 4th 905; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 274; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1263; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9339; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16022



December 16, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 17, 1994 (S037642), Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1345.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 20 Cal. App. 4th 1716 and 26 Cal. App. 645.)] (See 9 Cal. 4th 763)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. 61839, Herbert W. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs to County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, county residents and others, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Napa County (California), which declared that a local initiative confirming portions of the general plan relating to agricultural land, adopted by voters of defendant county, was valid, and dismissed a petition for writ of mandate. Plaintiffs contended that the initiative was not a valid use of the initiative power.


OVERVIEW: The residents of defendant county adopted an initiative that confirmed existing portions of the general plan relating to agricultural land, ensuring that those portions of the plan could not be changed without a vote of the people. Plaintiffs, county residents and others, sought a declaration that the initiative was invalid, and a writ of mandate ordering defendant's board of supervisors to cease enforcing the measure. The court affirmed a trial court judgment declaring the initiative to be valid and dismissing the petition for writ of mandate. The general plan affected a mixture of statewide and local concerns, and was amendable through the initiative power. Cal. Gov't Code § 65358 did not give defendant's board power to amend the general plan, but only provided procedures for exercising that power. Accordingly, the initiative did not interfere with any duty of defendant's board of supervisors to amend the general plan. The initiative did not intrude in matters preempted by state legislation, and did not frustrate land use planning in defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment declaring that a local initiative adopted by voters of defendant county, which confirmed portions of the general plan relating to agricultural land, was valid was affirmed, against plaintiffs', county residents and others, complaint. Defendant's board of supervisors did not have a statutory duty to amend the general plan, so the initiative did not interfere with the performance of any such duty.

People v. Baker


No. G013054.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


35 Cal. App. 4th 1413; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 372; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1257; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9354; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16013



December 15, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 11, 1994. Review Granted March 24, 1994.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 20 Cal. App. 4th 1700 and 26 Cal. App. 4th 629 and 31 Cal. App. 4th 889.)] Review Dismissed September 14, 1995 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 4th Dist., Div. 3 (rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5804.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-91738, Hugh Michael Brenner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that convicted and sentenced appellant for robbery, assault with great bodily injury, battery with serious bodily injury, and simple battery. The jury also found appellant committed the offenses because of the victim's race, color, religion, nationality, or country of origin, in violation of Cal. Penal. Code § 422.75 (b).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted and sentenced for robbery, assault with great bodily injury, battery with serious bodily injury, and simple battery. The jury also found he committed the offenses because of the victim's race, color, religion, nationality, or country of origin in violation of Cal. Penal. Code § 422.75 (b). The appeals court affirmed, holding that the trial court's jury instructions were not erroneous and evidence was not improperly admitted in the trial. The court held that the enhancement appellant received for the "hate crime" did not violate appellant's rights under U.S. Const. amend. I, and was not unconstitutional. The trial court did not err when it failed to sua sponte define terms in the jury instruction because the statute was not unconstitutionally vague, misleading, nor incorrect. There was no sua sponte duty to instruct on the meaning of terms in common usage, which are presumed to be within the understanding of persons of ordinary intelligence. The granting of prosecutor's motion to show co-defendant's tattoos to the jury did not violate the Confrontation Clause, U.S. Const. amend. VI, as the trial court gave a proper limiting instruction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the superior court that convicted and sentenced appellant for robbery, assault with great bodily injury, battery with serious bodily injury, and simple battery and found that appellant committed the offenses because of the victim's race, color, religion, nationality, or country of origin, holding that the enhancement appellant received for the "hate crime" did not violate appellant's rights to free speech.

Howard Jarvis Taxpayers' Assn. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. C009066. , No. C009490.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


20 Cal. App. 4th 1598; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1343; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9260; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15833



December 14, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV362874, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and the order after judgment awarding attorney fees are reversed and the matter is remanded with the following directions. The superior court shall, upon appropriate application, reconsider the issue of an award of attorney's fees in light of this opinion. The superior court shall render a judgment consisting of the declaration: "Any retail transactions and use tax ordinance under the Orange County Regional Justice Facilities Act ( Gov. Code, § 26295 et seq.) or, with the exception of an ordinance limited to 'any prevention program, as defined in Section 26299.011,' any retail transaction and use tax ordinance under the County Regional Justice Facilities Financing Act ( Gov. Code, § 26299.000 et seq.) is invalid if it is not approved by at least two- thirds of the county's voters, as required by section 4, article XIIIA." The parties shall bear their own costs of this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant counties and intervenor state board of equalization sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), declaring that the Orange County Regional Justice Facilities Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26295 et seq., and the County Regional Justice Facilities Financing Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26299.000 et seq., were unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, also known as Proposition 13.


OVERVIEW: Defendant counties and the intervenor state board of equalization challenged the trial court's declaration that two ad valorem tax acts, the Orange County Regional Justice Facilities Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26295 et seq., and the County Regional Justice Facilities Financing Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26299.000 et seq., were unconstitutional attempts to evade the dictates of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, also known as Proposition 13. As originally passed, the acts required approval by a simple majority of voters. The trial court held that Proposition 13 required approval by a two-thirds majority of voters. While the case was pending appeal, the legislature amended the statutes to require a two-thirds majority vote for passage; subsequently, both proposed acts were soundly defeated at the polls. On appeal, the court agreed that the acts, as originally drafted, violated Proposition 13. However, as amended, they did not. The court reversed and remanded the trial court's award of attorney fees to plaintiff taxpayer association and directed the trial court to enter an order specifying that future changes to the proposed acts require passage by a two-thirds majority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision holding that two legislative acts intended to raise ad valorem taxes to build new detention and courthouse facilities were unconstitutional because, since issuance of the trial court's decision, the proposed acts had been amended to comply with the constitutional mandate of Proposition 13 by requiring a two-thirds majority vote for passage.

United Auto Workers v. Department of Transportation


No. B069651.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


20 Cal. App. 4th 1462; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 290; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1240; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9213; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15766



December 13, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:   [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Part II of this opinion is not certified for publication. (Cal. Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1.)


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied March 17, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1367.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS011739, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order that Caltrans consider the UAW's request for benefits under title 25, section 6090 is affirmed. The award of fees and costs is reversed. Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant California Department of Transportation appealed a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that ordered defendant's Relocation Appeals Board to reconsider plaintiff property owner's appeal for renovation costs, pursuant to the provisions of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 25, § 6090(b)(1), and disregarding Cal. Code Regs. tit. 25, § 6018 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 21, § 1407.05, in a condemnation action.


OVERVIEW: Defendant California Department of Transportation denied the claim of plaintiff property owner for relocation assistance for renovating replacement property to make it suitable for plaintiff's use and the Relocation Appeals Board (Board) agreed. The trial court ruled that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 25, § 6018 precluded award of renovation costs if a project was federally funded, and thus violated Cal. Gov't Code §§ 7267.8, and 7260.5 and constituted an unconstitutional denial of equal protection. The trial court also ruled that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 21, § 1407.05 was invalid because it did not permit the exercise of discretion. The trial court ordered the Board to reconsider plaintiff's appeal pursuant to the provisions of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 25, § 6090(b)(1), and disregarding tit. 25, § 6018 and tit. 21, § 1407.05. On appeal, the court found that both § 7267.8 and tit. 25, § 6018 provided that, on federally assisted projects, federally allowed payments were the minimum to be paid. The court rejected the finding of unconstitutionality or conflict regarding tit. 25, § 6018. The court affirmed the order that the Board consider plaintiff's request for benefits under tit. 25, § 6090.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the result reached by the trial court in requiring reconsideration of the request of plaintiff property owner for renovation costs, by the Relocation Appeals Board of defendant California Department of Transportation. Defendant's regulation excluding payment for renovation costs was invalid, since it conflicted with guidelines requiring a displacing agency to exercise discretion with regard to those costs.

Amwest Sur. Ins. Co. v. Wilson


No. B058329.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


40 Cal. App. 4th 225; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 611; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1225; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9059; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15559



December 8, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 12, 1993. Second Modification January 7, 1994. Review Granted February 24, 1994 (S037405), Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 701.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 20 Cal. App. 4th 1275 and 26 Cal. App. 4th 361 and 31 Cal. App. 4th 822 and 35 Cal. App. 4th 1355.)] (See 11 Cal. 4th 1243)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C704879, Dzintra I. Janavs, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings as appropriate. Appellants Commissioner Garamendi and intervener Voter Revolt are awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant insurance commissioner and intervenor citizens' group sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their petition for a writ of mandate and for declaratory relief declaring that the state legislature violated the state constitution and Proposition 103 (the proposition) when it enacted Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.135 as an amendment to the proposition.


OVERVIEW: State voters passed Proposition 103 (the proposition), which required the rollback of insurance rates, the maintenance of fair insurance rates, and the election of a state insurance commissioner. Plaintiff surety company sought a writ of mandate in the trial court to stay the enforcement of the proposition. Subsequently, the legislature enacted an amendment to the initiative, Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.135, which provided for the exemption of surety companies from the proposition's rate regulations. Intervenor citizens' group filed a complaint in intervention for declaratory relief and petition for writ of mandate. Intervenor and defendant insurance commissioner argued that the assembly bill violated the state constitution and the proposition. The trial court denied the petition, but the appellate court reversed and remanded the judgment. The court held that the amendment was invalid because it did not comply with the initiative's requirement that it further the purposes of the proposition. The court found that within the plain meaning of the proposition, surety companies were subject to its requirements.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded a judgment that denied a petition for writ of mandate and for declaratory relief filed by defendant insurance commissioner and intervenor citizens' group after the legislature enacted an amendment which exempted surety companies from the requirements of Proposition 103. The court held that the amendment was invalid because it did not comply with the requirement that it further the purposes of the proposition.

 

961 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Diaz


No. D013341


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


20 Cal. App. 4th 1257; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 220; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1229; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9080; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15532



December 7, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 24, 1994 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR116825, Charles L. Patrick, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, San Diego County, (California) for burglary that also ordered that he comply with restitution orders issued under Cal. Gov't Code § 13967(c) requiring that defendant pay damages to the victim. The amounts due under the restitution order were to be taken from defendant's prison wages.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of burglarizing a home and was ordered to pay restitution by the trial court pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 13967. Defendant appealed the trial court judgment, contending that the restitution orders were invalid and unconstitutional. The court agreed with the state's argument that a former penal code section pertained to § 13967(a), and not § 13967(c) as relied upon by the trial court, and struck that part of the trial court judgment. Defendant primarily contended on appeal that one victim's loss, damage to a car transmission, was not caused by his crime, thus the restitution order for that was neither rehabilitative nor directly related to his crime. To impose restitution under § 13967(c), the economic loss suffered by the victim had to be the result of a defendant's criminal conduct. The trial court found that the victim's attempt to flee the danger of the burglary caused the transmission problems; thus, the transmission damage was a "result" of the criminal conduct and so recoverable under § 13967. The trial court judgment was affirmed as modified.


OUTCOME: The trial court judgment convicting the defendant of burglarizing the victims and ordering restitution was affirmed by the court, as modified. The court found that the restitution orders were based upon the wrong statutory provisions, and corrected the orders so that they were supported by the proper statute allowing imposition of restitution on defendants for economic losses suffered by the victim as a result of the criminal conduct.

 

962 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Brown


No. B072240.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


20 Cal. App. 4th 1251; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 76; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1223; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9042; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15495



December 7, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA055490, J. Stephen Czuleger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, including the state prison sentence of 15 years and 8 months, is otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for making terrorist threats under Cal. Penal Code § 422, after he approached two minority pedestrians with a gun, and threatened to kill them if they called the police. Appellant contended that he did not violate the statute because his threats were not unconditional. Appellant did not challenge his convictions on several other charges.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was involved in a heated argument with an individual when he noticed that two minority pedestrians, who were walking to their apartment, were watching the argument. Appellant approached the pedestrians, brandished a gun, and began shouting racial slurs at them. When one of the pedestrians mentioned calling the police, appellant stated that if they called the police he would kill them. Appellant was convicted for several offenses from the incident, but challenged his conviction for making terrorist threats under Cal. Penal Code § 422. Appellant contended that the statute prohibited only unconditional threats and therefore he did not violate the statute. The court agreed and reversed appellant's conviction for making terrorist threats. The court held that appellant's threat was not unequivocal or unconditional, and the plain meaning of the statute required that the threat contain no conditions. The court held that appellant's threat was based on the condition of calling the police, and therefore he did not violate the statute. The court affirmed appellant's sentence and other convictions imposed from the incident.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction for making terrorist threats after he approached two minority pedestrians with a gun and threatened to kill them if they called the police because the statute proscribed only unconditional threats, and appellant's threat was conditioned on calling the police, and therefore did not violate the statute. The court affirmed appellant's sentence and various other convictions imposed from the incident.

 

963 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Rodriguez


No. G012327.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


21 Cal. App. 4th 232; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 660; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1294; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9546; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16341



November 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published December 21, 1993. Review Denied March 3, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1205.
Review denied by People v. Rodriguez, 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1205 (Cal., Mar. 2, 1994)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Rodriguez v. Clark, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 14163 (E.D. Cal., Feb. 18, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-84891, David O. Carter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed and the matter is remanded to the trial court for resentencing and correction of the abstract of judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Orange County (California) which convicted him of second-degree murder. He argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress a photograph, obtained during an allegedly illegal "gang sweep" field interrogation, and subsequent identifications. Appellant also contended that the trial court erroneously imposed the aggravated term for gun use.


OVERVIEW: Obtaining information for a "gang book" to use in future criminal investigations, police approached defendant and his companions and ordered them to "stay there" while they were patted down for weapons. Officers interviewed them one at a time and took individual photos. Defendant was later identified from his gang book photo as the shooter in a murder. Witnesses also identified defendant from his booking photos and made in-court identifications. Denying defendant's motion to suppress the gang book photo and subsequent identifications, the trial court convicted him of second-degree murder. On appeal, the court affirmed. The investigatory stop of defendant and his companions based on suspected gang membership violated U.S. Const. amend. IV, the court held, because mere membership in a gang was not a crime. The court concluded that the gang book photo was fruit of the poisonous tree and should have been suppressed, but found that the booking photos and in-court identifications had independent bases for admission as witness recollections. Finding that defendant's "reracking" of his pistol to correct a misfire was not an aggravating circumstance, the court remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: Although finding that the gang book photo should have been suppressed as the fruit of an unconstitutional detention, the court found sufficient admissible evidence to affirm defendant's conviction for second-degree murder. The court remanded for resentencing because the trial court improperly imposed an aggravated circumstance for defendant's gun use.

 

964 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Allen


No. B074134.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


20 Cal. App. 4th 846; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 26; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1193; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8800; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15001



November 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 2, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1112.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 90P12442, Jessica Perrin Silvers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The People are awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted appellant of three counts of failure to file a state personal income tax return in violation of Cal. Rev. Tax. Code § 19401(a), and sentenced appellant to jail.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted in the trial court of three counts of misdemeanor failure to file a state personal income tax return in violation of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19401(a), and sentenced to jail. The appellate court affirmed and certified the matter to the court in order to settle the question of whether willfulness was an element of § 19401(a). The court held that because the collection of taxes served a high public purpose, tax statutes were therefor regulatory or public welfare in nature. Further, the fact that the sentence imposed by § 19041(a) was punitive rather then regulatory did not change that conclusion, as the determination of whether a statute was a strict liability offense did not turn on the type of punishment contained in the statute. Finally, because a violation of § 19041(a) constituted a public welfare offense, a finding of criminal intent was not required before someone could be convicted of that offense. Accordingly, the legislature created a strict liability offense by specifically providing in § 19041(a) that a person who failed to file a tax return was guilty of a misdemeanor even if that person had no intent to evade the law. Judgment affirmed.


OUTCOME: Judgment of conviction affirmed where because the tax statute under which appellant was convicted was a strict liability statute, whether or not appellant willfully violated that statute was inconsequential to a conviction thereunder.

People v. Swain


No. A058686.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


39 Cal. App. 4th 1658; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 598; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1185; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8743; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14919



November 24, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted March 17, 1994 (S037138), Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1338.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 20 Cal. App. 4th 495 and 25 Cal. App. 4th 1451 and 30 Cal. App. 4th 1461 and 35 Cal. App. 4th 327.)] (See 12 Cal. 4th 593)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 140954, Douglas C. Munson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction and sentences are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state, on cross-appeal, challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), where defendants were convicted for conspiracy to commit second-degree murder, and had been sentenced to 15 years to life. The state contended that under Cal. Penal Code § 182 every conspiracy to commit murder was required to be punished as a first-degree murder, with a sentence of 25 years to life.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of conspiracy to commit second-degree murder and other crimes, after the death of a young boy in a drive-by shooting. On cross-appeal the state contended that the trial court had improperly sentenced defendants to an indeterminate sentence of only 15 years to life for conspiracy to commit second-degree murder. The state asserted that under Cal. Penal Code § 182 every conspiracy to commit murder was to be punished as a first-degree murder, with a sentence of 25 years to life. The court affirmed. The trier of fact was authorized to return a verdict finding conspiracy to commit murder in the second degree. Only if the trier of fact failed to determine the degree, was a conspiracy to commit murder punished as one to commit first-degree murder. Since the legislature had authorized a verdict of conspiracy to commit second-degree murder, it clearly did not believe that crime to be a logical impossibility. The crime of conspiracy to commit second-degree murder was punishable under § 182 and Cal. Penal Code §190 by a sentence of 15 years to life.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' sentences of 15 years to life for conspiracy to commit second-degree murder, because the legislature had authorized a verdict of conspiracy to commit second-degree murder. Therefore, it did not believe that crime to be a logical impossibility.

Fisher v. County of Alameda


No. A059535.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


20 Cal. App. 4th 120; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 384; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1153; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8573; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14617



November 18, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 701725-0, James R. Lambden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), granting demurrer in favor of appellees, the City and the County, in appellants' action for a tax refund.


OVERVIEW: Appellants brought suit for declaratory relief and tax refund against the City and the County, alleging that the City's real estate transfer tax violated Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 and a related statutory provision, Cal. Gov't Code § 53725. The trial court sustained appellees' demurrer to the complaint. The court of appeals affirmed. Berkeley, Cal. Municipal Code § 7.52.040 et seq. imposed a tax of one and a half percent on the value of consideration for the real estate transfers. Appellants contended that Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 and Cal. Gov't Code § 53725 prohibited local real estate transfer taxes and required voter approval of local taxes. The City, however, was empowered to impose a real estate transfer tax, taking precedence over conflicting provisions of state law by virtue of the home rule powers conferred on charter cities by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5. The transfer tax had no impact outside the limits of the taxing municipality, but was purely local in its effects.


OUTCOME: The order granting the city and the county demurrer was affirmed because a real estate transfer tax did not violate the state constitution or a related statutory provision.

Sierra Club v. Department of Forestry & Fire Protection


Nos. A056250, A058664


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE.


20 Cal. App. 4th 14; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 487; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1142; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8475; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14482



November 15, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Withdrawn December 14, 1993. Substituted Opinion is Reported at: 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1336. (See 21 Cal. App. 4th 603)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Humboldt County, Nos. 82983 and 83428, William F. Ferroggiaro, Jr., and Roy G. MacFarland, * 


* Judge of the Glenn Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The trial court judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Humbolt County (California), which invalidated two of appellant's timber harvest plans for defendant's violation of Cal. Code Regs. tit. XIV, §§ 898,1, 898.2.


OVERVIEW: Appellant submitted two timber harvest plans (THP) to defendant for approval. Although the state's department of fish and game advised that certain mitigation measures designed to protect wildlife should be implemented before approval, defendant approved both THP's without requiring that those measures be contained in the THP's. Plaintiffs then filed suit against defendant and appellant seeking a writ of mandate and injunctions to stop logging at both sites. The trial court concluded that the THP's were invalid because defendant abused its discretion in approving them, and appellant sought the court's review. The court held that under Cal. Code Regs. tit. XIV, §§ 898.1, 898.2, defendant could not approve any THP unless all feasible measures necessary to substantially lessen any potential significant environmental impacts had been included in the THP. Therefore, because there was no evidence submitted at trial that the mitigation measures were unfeasible, and because defendant did not follow the applicable law as contained in §§ 898.1, 898.2, defendant's approval of the THP's constituted a prejudicial abuse of discretion. Accordingly, the trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Judgment in plaintiff's favor approved where because defendant did not follow the applicable law when it approved appellant's timber harvest plans, defendant's approval of those plans constituted an abuse of defendant's discretion.

Santa Clara County Local Transportation Authority v. Guardino


No. H010835.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


34 Cal. App. 4th 858; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 854; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1135; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8384; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14301



November 10, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 3, 1993. Review Granted January 27, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 293.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 19 Cal. App. 4th 1792 and 24 Cal. App. 4th 1354 and 29 Cal. App. 4th 1130.)] (See 11 Cal. 4th 220)


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Writ Proceeding



DISPOSITION: The collateral estoppel motion of the Santa Clara County Local Transportation Authority is dismissed as moot. The petition of the Transportation Authority for a writ of mandate is denied. The Transportation Authority shall pay all costs of respondent Carl Guardino in these proceedings; with this exception each party shall bear his, her, or its own costs herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner transportation authority sought a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to sign certain bonds relating to a sales tax petitioner sought to impose for transportation projects.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner transportation authority filed a petition for peremptory writ of mandate directed to respondent to command him to sign certain bonds relating to a sales tax. The court denied the writ and held that the sales tax was in valid because less than two-thirds a majority of the residents approved it. The court held that petitioner was a special district pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, and thus, its taxing power was limited and any special tax was required to be approved by two-thirds majority vote of the residents. The court held that petitioner was a special district because it was an entity expressly provided for by statute and formally created by the county board of supervisors for the explicit purpose of imposing a sales tax for transportation projects enumerated in an expenditure plan. In addition, the transportation projects to be funded by the sales tax were for city or county purposes. The court also concluded that that another tax could have been used to fund the projects enumerated in petitioner's expenditure plan. In addition, petitioner's sales tax was a special tax because it was levied specifically to fund enumerated public transportation projects.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ and held that the sales tax was invalid because it was not approved by two-thirds a majority of residents of the county who voted at the election. The court held that petitioner transportation authority's taxing authority was limited under the state constitution. Petitioner was a special district and the tax it sought to impose was a special tax because it sought to fund a specific governmental project or program.

 

969 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jimenez


No. G012722.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


19 Cal. App. 4th 1175; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 137; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1086; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8088; 93 Daily Journal DAR 13752



October 27, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. 1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-92751, John J. Ryan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed the order from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted defendant's demurrer and dismissed the information charging defendant with violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1320.5, on the grounds that the statute did not apply to bail pending appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced on a felony, but was released on bail pending appeal. Defendant failed to appear when his conviction was affirmed and remittitur issued. An information was filed alleging violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1320.5 because defendant was charged with a felony, had been released on bail, and had willfully failed to appear as required in order to prevent the imposition of sentence. The trial court granted defendant's demurrer, holding that the statute did not apply to bail pending appeal. Upon appeal by the state, the court reversed the trial court, holding that in the context of the bail statutes and the applicable case law it was the intention of the statute that whether it was pre-conviction or post-conviction, failure to appear while released on bail when charged with a felony was punishable as a separate offense. The court held that flight before sentencing was indistinguishable from flight to avoid the imposition of sentence because to find otherwise resulted in a harsher punishment for a defendant who fled before conviction and was presumed innocent than for a defendant who fled after conviction and had been found guilty.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court granting defendant's demurrer because defendant's failure to appear while released on bail for a felony offense was a new, chargeable offense, regardless of whether the bail was granted for pre-conviction or post-conviction proceedings.

Domar Elec. v. City of Los Angeles


No. B073387


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


29 Cal. App. 4th 1073; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 857; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1064; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7950; 93 Daily Journal DAR 13545



October 26, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted January 20, 1994 (S036526).
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 19 Cal. App. 4th 1034 and 24 Cal. App. 4th 1297.)] (See 9 Cal. 4th 161)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS020805, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Appellant Domar Electric, Inc., is awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, computer corporation, appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate to the trial court to prevent defendant, the City of Los Angeles, from entering into a contract with intervenor, rival computer corporation, or any other party than itself.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, computer corporation, and intervenor, rival computer corporation, entered bids to perform a public works project for defendant, the City of Los Angeles. Plaintiff submitted the lower bid. However, defendant gave the project to intervenor because plaintiff failed to submit documentation that it had complied with an affirmative action program. Plaintiff filed a petition for a writ of mandate preventing defendant from entering into the contract. The trial court denied the petition, but the court of appeal reversed and remanded. The program exceeded defendant's charter and was void. The charter specifically stated that projects had to be given to the lowest and best responsible bidder. The failure to submit documentation did not make plaintiff's bid nonresponsive. The examples provided by defendant were cases were the charter specifically required compliance with its provisions. Rejection for nondisclosure of compliance with the program was not in the charter. Defendant could not link the case through the general police power sections of the charter because the specific statutory language in the charter concerning bids controlled over the general language.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal reversed and remanded the judgment of the trial court which denied plaintiff, computer corporation, a writ of mandate to prevent defendant, the City of Los Angeles, from entering into a contract with intervenor, rival computer corporation, after plaintiff entered the lowest bid for the contract. The affirmative action program on which defendant based its decision violated defendant's city charter and was void.
