Cox v. Superior Court


No. C016269.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


19 Cal. App. 4th 1046; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 751; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1112; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8282; 93 Daily Journal DAR 13606



October 26, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied February 10, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 623.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Shasta County, No. F932697, Donald R. Kennedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining the respondeat superior court from taking any further proceedings in Shasta County Superior Court No. F932697 except to dismiss the action. Upon this decision becoming final, the stay previously issued is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a writ of prohibition to vacate an order by respondent, Superior Court of Shasta County (California) denying his motion to dismiss after the municipal court denied him permission to file a motion to suppress evidence. Respondent held that it was not timely filed. Petitioner had been detained after real party in interest, the state, filed charges against him for possession of a controlled substance and related offenses.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, the state, filed charges against petitioner inmate for possession of a controlled substance and related charges of sale and transportation. At the preliminary hearing, petitioner orally stated that he intended to move to suppress alleged illegally obtained evidence. The municipal court denied the motion because respondent did not give proper notice under Shasta County Mun. Ct. R. 7.03(E) and Shasta County Mun. Ct. R. 10.03. Respondent superior court denied petitioner's motion to dismiss. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to vacate respondent's order. The court of appeal granted the writ holding that the local rules conflicted with Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(f), which allowed motions to suppress to be raised at preliminary hearings. Supporting case law did not require notice based on a reading of legislative intent. Thus, the two rules were inconsistent with the statute and invalid. For policy reasons, to hold otherwise would force defendants to anticipate potentially inadmissible evidence, which would chill their right to bring motions to suppress at preliminary hearings.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal granted petitioner inmate's request for a writ of prohibition which restrained respondent superior court from taking further action accept to dismiss the case. Real party in interest's, the state's, objection to the filing of a motion to suppress at a preliminary hearing should not have been granted. Local rules which required notice could not supercede statutory law which gave petitioner the right to file without notice.

 

972 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



County of San Diego v. Brown


No. D015543. , No. D018698.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


19 Cal. App. 4th 1054; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 819; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1062; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7929; 93 Daily Journal DAR 13481



October 22, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied January 13, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 165.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 568230, 596055 and 612752, Barbara T. Gamer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The portion of the judgment declaring the Short-Doyle funding allocation system constitutional is affirmed. The portions of the judgment declaring the Short-Doyle state hospital bed allocation system unconstitutional, ordering reallocation of beds, granting injunctive relief against defendants, and awarding plaintiff counties damages are reversed with directions to the superior court to enter judgment for defendants on those issues. The portions of the judgment involving violations of former Welfare and Institutions Code section 5600 and ordering the Department to promulgate regulations under the Administrative Procedure Act are reversed with directions to the superior court to dismiss as moot plaintiffs' claims bearing on those issues. The portion of the decision denying Fresno plaintiffs recovery under the federal Rehabilitation Act of 1973 is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, county and interveners, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), finding no constitutional violation in an allocation of mental health funds pursuant to the Short-Doyle Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5600 et seq. Defendants, state treasurer and interveners, appealed portions of the judgment declaring allocation of hospital beds unconstitutional and instituting a related order and injunction.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, county and interveners, filed suit claiming state mental health department's (department) allocations of funding and state hospital beds violated equal protection and due process, and that defendants, state treasurer and interveners, violated Short-Doyle Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. 5600 et seq., and Administrative Procedure Act (APA), Cal. Gov. Code § 11342. The trial court found no constitutional violation in allocation of mental health funds pursuant to the Act. The court did find the department's allocation of state hospital beds unconstitutional and ordered reallocation, granted injunctive relief against defendants, and awarded plaintiffs damages. On appeal, the reviewing court found that an unequal distribution of state mental health resources resulted from historical choices by counties about participating in the Act's incentive matching program. Defendant's had a legitimate governmental purpose in maintaining a county's base allocation of state hospital beds while attempting to equitably reallocate resources over time. That interest was the stability of patient care and program infrastructure. Judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions.


OUTCOME: Judgment declaring funding allocation constitutional was affirmed. Judgment declaring state hospital bed allocation unconstitutional, ordering reallocation of beds, granting injunctive relief against defendants, state treasurer and interveners, and awarding damages to plaintiffs, counties and interveners, were reversed with directions. Defendants had a legitimate state interest in maintaining stability in patient care and program infrastructure.

 

973 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Friends of H Street v. City of Sacramento


No. C013852.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


20 Cal. App. 4th 152; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 607; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1158; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8597; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14692



October 21, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication November 18, 1993. As Modified on Denial of Rehearing November 18, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1155.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV525210, Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff residents association and plaintiff property owners appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which sustained defendant city's demurrer and dismissed plaintiffs' nuisance claim against defendant and held that plaintiffs could not amend their complaint to allege other claims against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff residents association and plaintiff property owners filed a nuisance complaint that sought an injunction to force defendant city to reduce the traffic speed and volume on a certain street. The trial court granted defendant's demurrer to the complaint because it found that routing of traffic was a legislative function and that, therefore, it did not have the power to grant the relief requested. The court affirmed the dismissal and held that plaintiffs did not state a cause of action because Cal. Civ. Code § 3482 stated that nothing done under the express authority of a statute could be deemed a nuisance and because courts lacked the power to compel legislative bodies to perform legislative acts in a certain manner. The court noted that defendant's authority to maintain and manage the street was defined in several statutes. The court also affirmed the ruling that plaintiffs could not amend their complaint to claim other relief because the complaint did not, as alleged by plaintiffs, state facts sufficient to state causes of action for inverse condemnation, dangerous condition of public property, or inconsistency with defendant's general plan.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that dismissed plaintiff residents association and plaintiff property owners' nuisance claim against defendant city and held that plaintiffs could not amend their complaint to allege other claims against defendant. The court held that the remedy sought by plaintiffs directly interfered with defendant's legislative function and that the trial court lacked the power to grant the requested relief.

 

974 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


City and County of San Francisco v. County of San Mateo


No. A057765.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


34 Cal. App. 4th 441; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 682; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1035; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7761; 93 Daily Journal DAR 13172



October 18, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted January 27, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 294.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 19 Cal. App. 4th 596 and 24 Cal. App. 4th 681 and 29 Cal. App. 4th 853.)] (See 10 Cal. 4th 554)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, Nos. 132-479, 133-826 and 137-403, Richard H. Breiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a judgement from the Superior Court of Marin County (California) that denied its claim for partial refund from appellee tax collectors. Appellant claimed that the newly passed Cal. Const. art. XIII A, Proposition 13, with its lower tax base calculation for real property, meant that appellant was no longer required to pay tax based upon the higher tax base calculation contained in Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 11.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer was a county that owned real property in the neighboring counties of appellee tax collectors. Appellant had paid real property tax based upon the calculation provided in Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 11. After the passage of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, also known as Proposition 13, appellant sought a partial tax refund based upon a claim that the lower tax base calculation in Proposition 13 applied to its property. The court affirmed the judgment to deny the partial refund claim. The court held that Proposition 13 had not expressly repealed or in any way altered § 11. The court relied upon principles of constitutional construction and determined first that Proposition 13 never remotely or explicitly mentioned § 11, evidencing voter intent that Proposition 13 did not impliedly repeal § 11. Next, the court noted that the legislature had considered this exact issue following the passage of Proposition 13, and yet concluded that government land was to continue to be taxed under § 11. Finally, the court held that as a rule the provisions in § 11 that specifically applied to government land controlled over the general real property tax provisions of Proposition 13.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied appellant taxpayer's claim for partial refund of real property taxes paid to appellee tax collectors because the newly enacted Proposition 13 applied to residential homeowners as a form of taxpayer relief and not to local governments, like appellant, that owned real property in other counties and that were subject to the specific tax calculation for such local government real property owners.

 

975 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Helmer v. Miller


No. D017661.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


19 Cal. App. 4th 1565; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 8; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1124; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8348; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14192



October 14, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published November 8, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 640548, William C. Pate, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Section 12021 does not deny Helmer due process nor equal protection and has not been preempted by federal law. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sustained without leave to amend the demurrer of respondents, regarding the constitutionality of a statute that makes it a felony for a previously convicted felon to be in possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought a declaration that the statute making it a felony for a person previously convicted of a felony to possess a firearm, Cal. Penal Code § 12021, subdiv. (a), was unconstitutional. He alleged that the section deprived him of due process of law under the United States and California Constitutions, deprived him of the right to bear arms, and that the regulation of possession of firearms by felons had been preempted by federal law. The court sustained without leave to amend the demurrer of respondents. Judgment was entered on the order sustaining the demurrer. On appeal, the court affirmed, because it found that the crime of a felon in possession of a firearm did not deny appellant due process or equal protection and the regulation of firearms by felons was within the historic police powers of the state and had not been preempted by federal law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the demurrer of respondents to appellant's challenge to the constitutionality of the statute that makes it a felony for a previously convicted felon to be in possession of a firearm, because the statute did not deny appellant due process of the law or equal protection and had not been preempted by federal law.

 

976 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Adams


No. H009744


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


19 Cal. App. 4th 412; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 512; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1020; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7600; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12921



October 12, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied January 13, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 178.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 147219, Robert Michael Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to stay the three-year section 667.8 enhancement on count 4. The clerk of the superior court is ordered to correct the abstract of judgment and to forward a corrected copy to the Department of Corrections. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted defendant of multiple counts of sexual assault with various enhancements and sentenced defendant to a term of 59 years in a state prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence for sexual assault. Defendant contended that his conviction should have been reversed because it was based on false evidence, but any false evidence admitted was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant challenged the validity of Cal. R. Crim. P. § 868.5. He argued that allowing prosecution witnesses in cases involving assaults to be attended by two support persons was inherently prejudicial. The court rejected the proposition that such a procedure deprived defendant of a fair trial, but found that the practice had an effect on jury observation of demeanor. The court, however, determined that the effect of the accompanying witness was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant contended that the trial court's instruction on acting in concert misstated the law by implying that acting in concert was synonymous with aiding and abetting. The jury instructions did not equate the two. Defendant also challenged the trial court's sentencing enhancements. The court agreed with defendant that only a single enhancement could be applied to the one incident. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, but modified his sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction but modified his sentence because the trial court improperly imposed multiple sentence enhancements for one incident.

 

977 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hawthorne Savings & Loan Assn. v. City of Signal Hill


No. B066509


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


19 Cal. App. 4th 148; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 272; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1007; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12633; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7475



October 4, 1993, Decided  
October 4, 1993


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Modified on Denial of Rehearing October 28, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1075. Review Denied December 30, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6921.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. LASC NS001703, Anita Shapiro, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying a peremptory writ of mandate is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court to enter a new and different judgment granting the writ and directing the City of Signal Hill to vacate its demolition order and to conduct such further proceedings on this matter as it may deem advisable in the manner required by law and consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's peremptory writ of mandate to vacate its order that sustained respondent city's decision to demolish residential buildings that appellant owned.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied appellant bank's peremptory writ of mandate to vacate its order that allowed respondent city to demolish appellant's residential buildings. The court reversed that denial on appeal and instructed the trial court to vacate its order and to hold further proceedings. The court found that respondent's acceptance of appellant's challenge of its decision to demolish the buildings and its holding a hearing on that challenge waived its claim that the challenge's timing and form were defective. The court also determined that appellant indirectly raised the issue of its right to repair by expressing its willingness to spend the estimated amount that was needed to correct the building code violations in its petition for a writ of mandate. The court further held that respondent had due process and statutory duties to afford appellant, even though it obtained the buildings subsequent to their being condemned, the choice of repairing or demolishing the buildings, a reasonable time to make that choice, and a reasonable opportunity to do so. The court also held that there were no exigent circumstances that required a more immediate demolition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a denial of appellant bank's peremptory writ of mandate to vacate an order that allowed respondent city to demolish its residential buildings and ordered further proceedings. The court concluded that respondent's hearing on appellant's challenge waived a claim as to its validity and found that due process and a statute gave respondent the option of demolishing the buildings or implementing a reasonable repair plan.

People v. Gear


No. D016208


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


19 Cal. App. 4th 86; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 261; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 994; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7402; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12546



October 1, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 29, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6849.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR124684, Timothy W. Tower, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of one count of the crime of continuous sexual abuse of a child under Cal. Penal Code § 288.5 and sentenced him to the middle term of 12 years in prison in a case brought by respondent, the people of California.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal challenged his conviction of one count of the crime of continuous sexual abuse of a child under Cal. Penal Code § 288.5. Appellant contended that § 288.5 was unconstitutional because it violated his right to jury unanimity on the actus reus of the crime. The court held that there was no constitutional infirmity to the generic proof in § 288.5, and it did not violate the right to a unanimous verdict. Appellant contended that § 288.5 violated his right to due process by not requiring the prosecution to specify the acts of molestation that formed the basis of the charge. The court held that the officer's testimony was very similar to the testimony provided by the child at trial, and, coupled with the information, provided sufficient due process notice to appellant of the charge of engaging in a continuous course of sexual abuse. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant criminal's conviction for one count of the crime of continuous sexual abuse of a child under the California penal code in a case brought by respondent, the people of California. The court held that the code did not violate the right to a unanimous verdict, and the officer's testimony, coupled with the information, provided sufficient due process notice to appellant of the charge.

 

979 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Garcia


No. F018987


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


19 Cal. App. 4th 97; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 340; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 995; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7450; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12612



October 1, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. 1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and IV of the Discussion.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 16888, Dennis A. Cornell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The condition of probation in the judgment is modified to provide that appellant is not to associate with persons he knows to be users or sellers of narcotics, felons or ex-felons. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which conditioned his probation for a conviction of petty theft with a prior conviction on his undergoing narcotics testing and refraining from associating with any felons, ex-felons, or users or sellers of narcotics.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded no contest to one count of petty theft with a prior conviction, and he entered the same plea to single counts of driving with a suspended license under Cal. Veh. Code § 14601.2 and alcohol-related reckless driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23103.5 in an unrelated case. The trial court placed appellant on probation. The court affirmed that judgment as modified. The court held that it was valid to require appellant to undergo narcotics testing, but that barring him from associating with any felons, ex-felons, or users or sellers of narcotics was unconstitutionally overbroad. The court modified that condition to prohibit appellant from associating with persons that he knew to fall in one of the above categories. The court held that the trial court's condition as to felons, ex-felons, and narcotics users and sellers had to be narrowly drawn because it impinged on appellant's right of freedom of association under U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the modified terms of appellant's probation for his conviction for petty theft with a prior conviction. Requiring appellant to take narcotics tests was valid, but not allowing associations with felons, ex-felons, or users or sellers of narcotics impinged on his constitutional freedom of association and had to be narrowly drawn to bar appellant from associating with those that he knew had one of those attributes.

 

980 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Maniscalco v. Superior Court


No. G014164


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


19 Cal. App. 4th 60; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 322; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 984; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7365; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12481



September 30, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Petition denied by People v. Maniscalco, 2001 Cal. LEXIS 6298 (Cal., Sept. 12, 2001)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Maniscalco v. Busby, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 146911 (C.D. Cal., Aug. 25, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-57585, Kathleen E. O'Leary, Judge.
Maniscalco v. Superior Court of County of Orange, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 5660 (Cal., Dec. 12, 1991)



DISPOSITION: The petition for a writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of mandate after the Superior Court of Orange County (California) denied him bail due to the capital nature of the charge against him, special circumstance murder, a charge which carried the possibility of a death penalty, but in which plaintiff state announced it would no longer seek the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was denied bail on the basis of the capital nature of the charge against him, special circumstance murder, a charge which carried the possibility of a death penalty, although plaintiff state had announced it would no longer seek the death penalty. Defendant sought a writ of mandate, which the court denied, holding that bail was precluded as long as the defendant was charged with a capital offense, even if he was not actually in jeopardy of the death penalty. The court distinguished between rights which depended on whether there was a capital "crime" versus rights that depended on whether there was a capital "case," looking to the purpose behind the rights. The court held that the core reason for denying bail to defendant was related directly to the gravity of his "crime," and had no necessary relationship to the complexity of defendant's "case" or his potential punishment, whereas rights to extra investigators and experts, and similar rights, which defendant would have if he were still facing the possibility of the death penalty, would bear directly on his "case" and potential punishment.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for a writ of mandate, holding that defendant was not entitled to bail despite the fact that plaintiff state would no longer seek the death penalty, because the right to bail depended upon whether defendant was charged with a capital "offense," not upon whether his was a capital "case," because the right to bail was related to the gravity of the crime, not to the potential punishment.

People v. Calvert


No. D016412.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


18 Cal. App. 4th 1820; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 644; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 977; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7314; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12408



September 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 16, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6462.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR123138, Norbert Ehrenfreund, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of manufacturing, possessing for sale and transporting methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11379.6(a), 11379, and 11378, and convictions of conspiracy to manufacture, transport and possess methamphetamine for sale, in violation of §§ 11379.6(a), 11379, 11378, and Cal. Penal Code § 182(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant had an automobile repair shop. With the consent of an employee, and pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 2805, police entered the shop to check for stolen parts. An officer went entered an office area looking for the owner, and saw a mound of powder on a mirror on the floor. The officer smelled marijuana and saw two snorting straws next to the powder, which turned out to be methamphetamine. The trial court denied the defendant's motion to suppress, and the court affirmed. Defendant argued that because § 2805 only authorized inspections of vehicles and their parts, etc., the officer had no authority to enter the office area. The court held that it was reasonable for the police to attempt to inform the owner of their presence, and thus the officer had viewed the methamphetamine from a place where he was lawfully present. In response to defendant's argument that § 2805 was unconstitutional because warrantless administrative inspections were permitted only of closely regulated businesses, the court pointed out numerous regulations relating to automobile repair to show that the automobile repair industry was closely regulated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, because, given the closely regulated nature of the automobile repair business, the warrantless search was constitutional as an administrative inspection, and the officer who observed the methamphetamine was legitimately in the office area seeking to notify the owner of the inspection.

 

982 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hansen v. Workers' Compensation Appeals Bd.


No. A061777.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


18 Cal. App. 4th 1179; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 30; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 958; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7133; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12077; 58 Cal. Comp. Cases 602



September 22, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied November 23, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6292.


PRIOR HISTORY: WCAB No. SRO 71448.



DISPOSITION: The order denying petitioner's claim for a psychiatric injury is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed from an order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), which denied petitioner's claim for psychiatric injury due to stress in a workers' compensation proceeding, holding that petitioner was not entitled to receive compensation because she had not been employed by her employer for six months as required by Cal. Lab. Code § 3208.3(d).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee filed a workers' compensation claim for a psychiatric injury due to stress while working as a waitress for a hotel. Respondent workers' compensation appeals board denied petitioner's claim, holding that she had not been employed by the hotel for at least six months as required by Cal. Lab. Code § 3208.3(d). On appeal, the court affirmed the denial of petitioner's claim, holding that the six-month requirement of § 3208.3(d) that applied to psychiatric injuries resulting from a regular and routine employment event did not violate petitioner's right to equal protection of the law under a rational basis review because the legislature's classification was not palpably arbitrary and irrational, and that § 3208.3 did not violate petitioner's right to due process. Further, the court held that the exclusive remedy provisions of the workers' compensation statutes, Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3601, 3602, did not violate petitioner's constitutional rights to equal protection of the law and due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of petitioner applicant's workers' compensation claim for a psychiatric injury due to stress, holding that the statutory requirement that petitioner be employed for at least six months in order to receive compensation for a psychiatric injury resulting from a regular and routine employment event did not violate her constitutional rights to equal protection of the law and due process.

People v. Heitzman


No. G012689.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


29 Cal. App. 4th 150; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 199; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 959; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7134; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12079



September 21, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted December 2, 1993 (S035624), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6384.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 18 Cal. App. 4th 1400, 23 Cal. App. 4th 1041.)] (See 9 Cal. 4th 189)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-88586, Luis A. Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed with respect to the Penal Code section 995 motion only and reversed with directions to overrule the demurrer and proceed in accordance with the views expressed above.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which sustained defendant's demurrer to the elder abuse charge against her on the grounds that the first clause of Cal. Penal Code § 368(a) was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad, and granted her Cal. Penal Code § 995 motion regarding charges filed under the other two clauses of § 368(a) on the grounds that she was not her father's caretaker.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had previously lived with her elderly, pensioner father and had been his caretaker. At the time of his death, defendant was no longer her father's caretaker. She was, however, a frequent visitor in his home and was present in the home at the time of his death. Following her father's death resulting from septic shock from bed sores, malnutrition, and neglect, defendant was charged with elder abuse under Cal. Penal Code § 368(a). The trial court granted defendant's motion under Cal. Penal Code § 995, holding that the charges under two clauses of § 368(a) could not stand because defendant was not her father's caretaker. The trial court also sustained defendant's demurrer, finding that the first clause of § 368(a) was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court agreed that defendant could not be prosecuted under the "caretaker" portions of § 368(a). However, the court ruled that the first clause of § 368(a) did apply to defendant and was not unconstitutional, at least as applied to an adult child who owed her father a duty of care under Cal. Civ. Code § 270c.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment dismissing one elder abuse charge against defendant, because the elder abuse statute was constitutional as applied to defendant where, as an adult child, she owed her father at least the minimal duty of care required to avoid gross negligence. The court affirmed the judgment that defendant could not be prosecuted under the portions of the statute aimed at caretakers as defendant was not her father's caretaker.

 

984 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Matthews


No. F017310


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


18 Cal. App. 4th 1064; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 434; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 944; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6996; 93 Daily Journal DAR 11837



September 16, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 2, 1993 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.). Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6404.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 12722, John P. Moran, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a certificate of rehabilitation and pardon pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4852.01(c).


OVERVIEW: As a resident of Oregon, appellant filed a petition in the trial court pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4852.01 et seq., for rehabilitation and pardon, which was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the three-year residency requirement of Cal. Penal Code § 4852.01(c) did not violate the equal protection clauses of either U.S. Const. amend. XIV or Cal. Const. art. V, § 8. The court distinguished between residency requirements and durational residency requirements, and found that only the latter were violative of the right to travel because they affected migration from state to state. On the other hand, the residency requirement set forth in § 4852.01(c) did not implicate a suspect classification or a fundamental right, and therefore was not subject to the strict scrutiny test that a durational residency requirement would be. The court determined that the residency requirement of § 4852.01(c) furthered the state's substantial interest in assuring that the services it provided for its citizens pertaining to the non-fundamental right to pardon were enjoyed only by its residents.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant's petition for a certificate of rehabilitation and pardon, because appellant, who resided in Oregon, did not meet the three-year residency requirement contained in the pardon statute, and the residency requirement did not violate appellant's equal protection rights as it furthered the state's substantial interest in providing services for its residents.

 

985 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Dunn-Edwards Corp. v. South Coast Air Quality Management Dist.


No. B057868


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


19 Cal. App. 4th 536; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 99; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 1039; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7782; 93 Daily Journal DAR 13257



September 16, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 15, 1993. Ordered Published October 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 753978, Ronald M. Sohigian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, paint manufacturers, contractors, and dealers, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained without leave to amend defendant technical review group's demurrers to several causes of action in plaintiff's complaint and then dismissed plaintiff's action, which challenged certain amendments to environmental regulations, as to defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, paint manufacturers, contractors, and dealers, filed suit against several parties, including defendant technical review group, challenging amendments to environmental regulations. Plaintiffs alleged that defendant usurped the rulemaking authority of air pollution control districts and thereby engaged in state action, which interfered with plaintiffs' contractual relations and denied them due process. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrers to several counts of plaintiffs' complaint and ultimately dismissed the complaint as to defendant. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that defendant had not engaged in state action when its members participated in the promotion and approval of the regulation amendments because its members merely acted in an advisory capacity to the districts, which was not in any way statutorily prohibited. The court found that the complaint failed to show that plaintiffs were deprived of notice and a hearing before the districts' subsequent adoption of the amendments. The court held that plaintiffs' remedy, if any, was against the districts because there was no unlawful delegation to or usurpation by defendant of the districts' powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sustaining defendant technical review group's demurrers several causes of action raised by plaintiffs, paint manufacturers, contractors, and dealers, and dismissing plaintiffs' complaint, because defendant did not usurp the rulemaking authority of the air pollution control districts when it acted in an advisory capacity to environmental regulation amendment and thus did not engage in state action.

 

986 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Howard Jarvis Taxpayers' Ass'n v. State Bd. of Equalization


Nos. C009066, C009490


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


18 Cal. App. 4th 812; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 330; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 914; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6714; 93 Daily Journal DAR 11407



September 2, 1993, Filed


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. CV362874. Hon. James T. Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, California Board of Equalization, and intervenor counties sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which declared the Orange County Regional Justice Facilities Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26295 et seq., and the County Regional Justice Facilities Financing Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26299.000 et seq., unconstitutional and granted an injunction prohibiting their implementation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a taxpayers' association and individual taxpayers, were granted judgment against defendant, California Board of Equalization, and intervenor counties, declaring that the Orange County Regional Justice Facilities Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26295 et seq., and the County Regional Justice Facilities Financing Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 26299.000 et seq., were unconstitutional. Defendant and intervenors were enjoined from entering into any agreement to administer the tax, and plaintiffs were granted attorney's fees. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the only relief warranted was a declaration that any retail transactions and use tax ordinances under § 26295 et seq., or § 26299.000 et seq., with the exception of an ordinance limited to "any prevention program" as defined in § 26299.011, were invalid if not approved by at least two-thirds of the county's voters, as required by Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 4, because any agencies created by the acts qualified as "special districts" within the meaning of art. XIIIA, § 4. The court held that injunctive relief was not appropriate, and the trial court was instructed to reconsider the issue of attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders granting declaratory relief, injunctive relief, and attorney's fees, and held that retail transactions and use tax ordinances to fund detention and courthouse facilities were invalid if not approved by at least two-thirds of the county's voters, but that injunctive relief was not appropriate.

 

987 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Glendale Redevelopment Agency v. Parks


No. B053099.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


18 Cal. App. 4th 1409; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 14; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 964; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7283; 93 Daily Journal DAR 12116



August 31, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published September 22, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 652971, George M. Dell, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the Los Angeles Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The Agency to have its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment for appellants and denied appellants' posttrial motions for litigation expenses, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1250.410, and interest, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 685.010(a), in an eminent domain proceeding by respondent Glendale Redevelopment Agency (California).


OVERVIEW: Respondent Glendale Redevelopment Agency (California) initiated an eminent domain proceeding against appellant property owners' building. Respondent appraised the property at $ 1.5 million, and appellants' appraised the property at $ 1.9 million. Respondent offered appellant $ 1.7 million to settle after respondent's counsel and its appraiser reviewed appellants' appraisal. A jury awarded appellants' $ 1.9 million, and appellants filed posttrial motions for litigation expenses, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1250.410, and interest, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 685.010(a). The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the superior court did not abuse its discretion in denying litigation expenses, as there was ample evidence that respondent acted in good faith in making the $ 1.7 million offer, and that appellants' argument that § 685.010(a) set the proper interest rate because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1268.350 was unconstitutional was without merit, as § 1268.350 was consistent with the requirement that the trial court determine appropriate rates of interest in condemnation proceedings. Appellants were only entitled to interest from the date that respondent took possession.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the superior court did not abuse its discretion in denying litigation expenses to appellant property owners in an eminent domain proceeding by respondent Glendale Redevelopment Agency (California), as respondent acted in good faith in making a settlement offer, and that the statute setting the interest rate was constitutional, as the trial court was required to determine the interest rate.

 

988 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Estate of Cleveland


No. B072638.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


17 Cal. App. 4th 1700; 22 Cal. Rptr. 2d 590; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 863; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6302; 93 Daily Journal DAR 10818



August 23, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied November 24, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6355.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BP011054, Arnold H. Gold, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Appellant shall bear respondent's costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adult foster child sought review of the summary judgment for respondent trustee in the suit appellant brought to claim a share of decedent's estate under Cal. Prob. Code § 6408(e) in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). The superior court ruled that appellant lacked standing because he had no rights of inheritance. Appellant claimed decedent would have adopted him but for the lack of his mother's consent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant adult foster child sought review of the summary judgment for respondent trustee in the suit appellant brought to share in decedent foster father's estate. Cal. Prob. Code § 6408(e) provided that a foster child in a continuous parent-child relationship could have shared in decedent's estate where the decedent would have adopted the foster child but for a legal impediment. Decedent was intestate, but his property had been transferred to a trust. Decedent specified a testamentary bequest to appellant, describing him as friend and godson. Appellant and his mother had lived with decedent for 12 years. There had been no marriage, and appellant's mother refused consent when decedent wanted to adopt him as a minor. Affirming the summary judgment against appellant, the court explained that if the decedent wished appellant to share in his estate, he could have adopted him during the 16 years when the mother's consent was not needed, or written a will providing more for him. The court added that once adoption became possible, the decedent should have been given a reasonable time to effectuate the adoption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment denying appellant adult foster child a share in the decedent's estate whose property was administered by respondent trustee. The court explained that appellant had not proved that decedent would have adopted him but for a legal impediment, as required by the statute, because the obstacle, the mother's consent, had not been required for the 16 years since appellant reached majority.

People v. Superior Court (Aishman)


No. D018536.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


32 Cal. App. 4th 1350; 22 Cal. Rptr. 2d 311; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 855; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6278; 93 Daily Journal DAR 10770



August 19, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted November 17, 1993 (S033133), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6024.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 17 Cal. App. 4th 1489 and 22 Cal. App. 4th 1030 and 27 Cal. App. 4th 1782.)] (See 10 Cal. 4th 735)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. ECR6713, Robert E. May, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the court to vacate its order of January 13, 1993, dismissing the section 422.75 allegations as unconstitutional, and to enter a new order consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought a writ of mandate to direct respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California) to vacate its order holding that Cal. Penal Code § 422.75(b), which provided enhanced penalty for those choosing a victim because of race or other specified reasons and acting in concert, violated U.S. Const. amend. IV and XIV. Real parties in interest were charged under Cal. Penal Code §§ 245(a)(1) and 243(d).


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest were charged with assault with a deadly weapon and by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 245(a)(1), and battery with serious bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 243(d). The state appealed after the superior court ruled that the penalty enhancement provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 422.75(b) violated U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend XIV. Issuing a peremptory writ of mandate, the court held § 422.75 was constitutional. The court explained that those who choose to employ conduct as a means of expression must make sure that the conduct they selected was not generally forbidden. Rejecting the argument that § 422.75 had an unconstitutional chilling effect on freedom of expression, the court said the punishment was not only on the basis of beliefs, and, except when relevant to a criminal act, they could not have been offered in evidence. The court found no overbreadth problem because § 422.75 only applied where the conduct was a felony and the state had proved a causal connection.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the superior court to vacate its order that the enhancement statute for defendants acting in concert and choosing their victim on the basis of race, color, religion, nationality, country of origin, ancestry, disability, or sexual orientation was unconstitutional. The court held the statute did not have a chilling effect and was not overbroad.

 

990 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Feminist Women's Health Center v. Blythe


No. C011874.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


17 Cal. App. 4th 1543; 22 Cal. Rptr. 2d 184; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 862; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6311; 93 Daily Journal DAR 10727



August 19, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication.* * 


* *Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I and II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied November 10, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5930.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 506341, James L. Long, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The attorney fee order is reversed as to defendants Walker and Operation Rescue. In all other respects, the judgment and attorney fee order are affirmed. Defendants Blythe, Lewis, Reali, Baggett and Stoos shall pay plaintiff's costs on appeal. Plaintiff shall pay the costs on appeal of defendants Walker and Operation Rescue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant abortion protestors sought review from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted a permanent injunction imposing time, place, and manner restrictions on the activities of appellants at respondent abortion clinic's premises, and an order awarding attorney fees was disputed. Appellants asserted various challenges to the propriety and scope of the injunction as well as the award of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant abortion clinic protestors caused disturbances that posed problems for patients trying to enter respondent abortion clinic's premises. Respondent brought an action seeking a permanent injunction against appellant's continued disruptive activities. Appellants sought review after the trial court issued the injunction and awarded attorney fees. On review, the court ruled that appellants' obstructing access to the clinic and engaging in acts of assault and harassment to prevent women who had expressed disinterest in appellants' views was not protected by the federal or state constitutions. The court held that the injunction was tailored narrowly to protect the patients' privacy interests, while at the same time providing appellants with ample opportunity to communicate their antiabortion message and was therefore appropriate. As to the attorney fees award, the court affirmed the award with exception to defaulting parties because respondent's complaint did not demand such attorney fees and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 580 precluded an award against a defaulting party which exceeded the relief demanded in the complaint. The decision was affirmed and reversed accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the permanent injunction against appellant protestors because the injunction was tailored narrowly to protect respondent abortion clinic's patients' privacy interest while providing appellants with ample opportunity to communicate their message. The court affirmed the attorney fee award as to the answering appellants but reversed as to defaulting parties where respondent's complaint did not demand attorney fees as required.

 

991 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


In re M. S.


No. A055072. , No. A055963.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


32 Cal. App. 4th 1320; 22 Cal. Rptr. 2d 560; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 839; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6177; 93 Daily Journal DAR 10596



August 17, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted November 17, 1993 (S035200), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6027.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 17 Cal. App. 4th 1328 and 22 Cal. App. 4th 988 and 27 Cal. App. 4th 1752.)] (See 10 Cal. 4th 698)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 116302 and 116304, Donald S. Mitchell, Douglas C. Munson, and Alex Saldamondo, Judges; Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 141289, Wilmont Sweeney, Judge, and Claudette Brooks, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants in consolidated cases appealed from judgments of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), and the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which found that each defendant committed assault with a deadly weapon, battery, and interference with the exercise of civil rights in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 422.6 (1987), and that the offenses were enhanced pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 422.7 (1987).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were found to have threateningly yelled antigay epithets at and physically attacked two homosexual men. Counts of assault with a deadly weapon, battery, and interference with the exercise of civil rights in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 422.6 (1987) were enhanced pursuant to the "hate crime" statute, Cal. Penal Code § 422.7 (1987). The court affirmed the judgments. Cal. Penal Code §§ 422.6 and 422.7 required proof of a specific intent to deprive a person of a defined federal right, and were not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. Cal. Penal Code § 422.7 did not infringe U.S. Const. amend. I guarantees because it did not punish speech as such, but enhanced the penalty where the perpetrator selected the victim on the basis of race or other protected status. Cal. Penal Code § 422.6 fell within exceptions to U.S. Const. amend. I prohibitions on content-based regulations of speech delineated by the United States Supreme Court. The statute was aimed at conduct, not the suppression of ideas, and only proscribed speech that threatened violence where the speaker had the apparent ability to carry out the threat, and the victim reasonably feared for his or her safety.


OUTCOME: The judgments convicting defendants of assault with a deadly weapon, battery, and interference with the exercise of civil rights were affirmed. The court held that the statutes under which the defendants were convicted were not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad, and did not infringe First Amendment guarantees of freedom of speech, where one was a mere enhancement, and the other punished conduct that threatened violence.

Yee v. Mobilehome Park Rental Review Bd.


Nos. D016072, D016083.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


17 Cal. App. 4th 1097; 23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 1; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 825; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6027; 93 Daily Journal DAR 10309



August 9, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. N51754 and N51755, Ronald S. Prager, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed. The superior court is instructed to issue a writ directing the Board to conduct a new rent increase hearing consistent with the dictates of this opinion. Each party will bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners sought review of a decision rendered by respondent board, in which respondent, pursuant to statutory authority, determined the amount of the rental rate increase plaintiffs were entitled to based upon a fair rate-of-return theory.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff owners filed applications with respondent board for rent increases; respondent conducted a hearing and determined the amount of the rental increases permitted. However, respondent failed to account for interest payments on plaintiffs' loans in determining the net income derived from the operation of the mobile parks and applied the fair rate-of-return theory to compute the rental increase. Plaintiffs appealed respondent's decision contending the rent control ordinance deprived plaintiffs of substantive and procedural due process and respondent's decision lacked supported by virtue of substantial evidence. The court reversed respondent's decision and remanded to the trial court directing it to issue a writ directing respondent to conduct a new hearing regarding the issue of a rental rate increase. The court held the ordinance did not deprive plaintiffs of their substantial and procedural due process rights. Further, the court held respondent's decision was not supported by substantial evidence, even though it may have reached the proper conclusion. The court reasoned respondent applied the wrong theory in making its decision; thus, a new hearing was necessary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of respondent board regarding a rental rate increase sought by plaintiff owners and remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a writ directing respondent to conduct a new hearing regarding the rate increase. The court held that although the same result could have been reached by applying a different theory to determine the rate increase, under substantial evidence review, reversal was required.

Conservatorship of Tedesco


No. A056167.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


27 Cal. App. 4th 1274; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 763; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 794; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5823; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9899



August 2, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted October 28, 1993 (S035032), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5755.
Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 17 Cal.App.4th 758 and 22 Cal.App.4th 662.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County, No. 8800, Robert L. Crone, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ward challenged the decision from the Superior Court of Lake County (California) which found her gravely disabled within the meaning of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5350 and appointing the public guardian as her conservator.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ward was involuntarily committed after being found gravely disabled within the meaning of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5350. Appellant lived in squalor. Appellant challenged the decision arguing that the exclusionary rule should have been applied to suppress evidence obtained in violation of her U.S. Const. amend. IV right, and that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that they could not consider evidence that appellant had friends and family to help her unless the offers of such assistance were presented in writing. The court found that the exclusionary rule did apply and applied to involuntary commitment proceedings due to the severe deprivations that someone involuntarily committed suffers. However, the court found that this violation was not reversible error because of the ample amount of other evidence. The court also found that the jury instruction requiring a writing, was supported by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5350(e)(2). The court therefore affirmed the commitment.


OUTCOME: The involuntary commitment of appellant ward was affirmed. The violation of the exclusionary rule was not reversible error because of the ample amount of other evidence to support the commitment, and because the trial court did not err in instructing the jury that only written evidence of assistance was to be considered.

Central Delta Water Agency v. State Water Resources Control Bd.


No. C014599.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


17 Cal. App. 4th 621; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 453; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 932



July 28, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 14, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5417.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 243391, Terrence R. Van Oss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, water agencies and a landowner, sought relief from the denial of a petition for mandamus and declaratory relief by the Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 243391 (California), to declare unconstitutional Cal. Water Code §13396.5 and to prevent enforcement of Cal. Code Regs., tit. 23, §2236, enacted by respondent State Water Resources Control Board. Appellants also challenged the trial court's decision that they lacked standing.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected respondent State Water Resources Board's contention that appellants, water agencies and a landowner, lacked standing to declare unconstitutional Cal. Water Code §13396.5 and to prevent enforcement of its implementing regulation Cal. Code Regs., tit. 23, §2236 because appellant water agencies were authorized by statutes to sue on behalf of their constituent water users. Appellants claimed that respondent violated both California and Federal equal protection because it construed §13396.5 to require fees only from direct dischargers, or if respondent's interpretation of §13396.5 was reasonable, §13396.5 itself violated equal protection. The court held that respondent's interpretation of §13396.5 was reasonable based on its legislative history and rules of statutory interpretation and that such interpretation did not violate equal protection because the distinction in §13396.5 bore a rational relationship to a conceivable legitimate state purpose. The court held §13396.5(e) did not violate Cal. Const. art. I, §10 although it imposed imprisonment for failure to pay a fee, as this was a reasonable interpretation of §13396.5(e) that avoided constitutional infirmity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling that appellants water agencies and a landowner lacked standing but affirmed the judgment of the trial court that denied appellants' petition for mandamus and declaratory relief to declare unconstitutional a certain water discharge statute and prevent enforcement of its accompanying implementing regulation. Appellants were denied relief because the statutory scheme was not unconstitutional.

Redevelopment Agency of San Ramon v. Sghayer


No. A056887, A057846


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 183; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 752; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5521; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9312



July 20, 1993, Filed


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Ordered Not for Publication August 13, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 832. Rehearing Denied August 13, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 832.


PRIOR HISTORY: Contra Costa County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. C90-04800. Hon. Ellen S. James.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners and agency, sought review of the summary judgment from the Contra Costa County Superior Court (California), which was granted in favor of appellee developers that found that the 25-acre provision in an ordinance was enacted before appellants purchased the property and was not actionable, appellants' challenge was time barred by Cal. Gov't Code § 65009(c), and that there was no evidence of a conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Following the summary judgment that was granted in favor of appellee developers and that found that the provision in an ordinance was enacted prior to appellant property owners' purchase of the property and that appellants' challenge was time barred by Cal. Gov't Code § 65009(c), appellants sought review of the decision. Appellants contended that the trial court erred in finding appellants' challenge time barred, that summary judgment was improper, and that appellees interfered with appellants right to receive the full fair market value of the property. Appellees cross-appealed, claiming the trial court improperly reduced the amount of attorney fees. The court affirmed, holding that there was no reason that the statute of limitation should have been declared inapplicable when appellants were on notice as to the existence of the ordinance prior to the purchase. Further, the court held that in light of the fact the statute of limitations barred all challenges to the ordinance, all of appellants' claims failed, and summary judgment was properly granted. Additionally, the court found that the evidence provided ample reason for the trial court to reduce the attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment granted in favor of appellee developers that found an ordinance was enacted before appellants purchased the property, was not actionable, and that awarded attorney fees was affirmed. The court held that the record showed that appellant property owners had notice and were given an opportunity to challenge the ordinance's validity. Further, there was no abuse of discretion in the amount of attorney fees awarded.

People v. Beck


No. H010102.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


17 Cal. App. 4th 209; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 250; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 735; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5434; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9156

DISPOSITION: The restitution order in counts 1 and 2 is reversed. In light of the computational discrepancy noted in count 2, and this court's determination that restitution amounts for monies taken prior to January 1, 1990, should not exceed $ 10,000, this matter is remanded to the trial court with direction to conduct appropriate proceedings to redetermine the correct restitution amounts in counts 1 and 2. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged the ruling of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which charged him with nine counts of grand theft.

Gov't Code §13960 and §13967 did not preclude direct restitution to the tax board. However, the court remanded the case to calculate the appropriate amount of restitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the ruling of the trial court only to the extent of recalculating restitution as to not exceed the total of $ 10,000 in conformance with statutory law.

 

997 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



California Ranch Homes Development Co. v. San Jacinto Unified School Dist.


No. E010693.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


17 Cal. App. 4th 573; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 557; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 778; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5718; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9684



July 13, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published July 28, 1993. Review Denied September 29, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5255.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 210673, Victor Miceli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellant shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which ordered it to refund school impact fees to respondent real estate developer that were erroneously paid at the residential rate instead of at the lower rate applicable to senior citizen housing.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was the owner and developer of a residential real estate project situated within the jurisdictional boundaries of appellant school district. Occupancy of the project was limited to individuals 55 years of age and older. Respondent initially paid school impact fees at the residential rate instead of at the lower rate applicable to senior citizen housing. Approximately a year after making the last payment, respondent learned that senior citizen housing projects could be assessed school impact fees at the lower commercial or industrial rate. Respondent brought an action for a refund of the alleged overpayment. The trial court entered judgment for respondent. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment, holding that the statute of limitations in Cal. Gov't Code § 66020(d) barred the action and was the exclusive method for challenging the imposition of the fees, or for challenging an overpayment of the fees. Respondent's challenge thus failed where respondent did not timely file its protest, and failed to bring an action within 180 days of the imposition of fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment ordering appellant school district to refund school impact fees, holding that respondent real estate developer did not timely file a protest to the imposition of the school impact fees, which was a mandatory prerequisite to any later action to challenge the imposition of the fees. Respondent also failed to file an action within 180 days after imposition of the fees.

 

998 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Oryx Energy Co. v. County of Kern


No. F016864.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


17 Cal. App. 4th 48; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 221; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 720; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5353; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9007



July 12, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. *


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of subpart B. of part I.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 200420, Lenard M. McGillivray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Costs to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which held that respondent taxpayer was entitled to a refund of certain property taxes paid on account of respondent's leasehold interest in three mineral leases granted to it by the federal government.


OVERVIEW: Respondent taxpayer held mineral leases from the federal government for which respondent paid royalties on the value of gross gas and oil production. The leaseholds were subject to county property tax. Respondent sought a reduction in assessed value, and a related tax refund, which appellant county refused. Respondent filed suit. The trial court held that the leaseholds were overvalued, and that respondent was due a refund. Appellant challenged, and the court reversed. The central issue was whether, under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107.2, the leasehold value, for assessment purposes, was to be reduced by the present value of royalties that respondent was obliged to pay. The court reviewed Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107.2, and concluded that even though the lessor government might not agree to reduce royalties to compensate for a higher effective tax rate, the royalties nonetheless were not being paid pursuant to "authority," which prohibited royalty rate reductions. As respondent's leases did not satisfy the statutory condition for excluding royalties from the leasehold value assessment, the court reversed, and entered judgment for appellant.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. Appellant county did not have to refund certain property taxes paid by respondent taxpayer on leasehold interests granted by the federal government, because the leases did not qualify for assessment by means of a method that excluded, from the leasehold value, the present value of royalties paid to the federal government.

 

999 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Binkley v. City of Long Beach


No. B069234


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


16 Cal. App. 4th 1795; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 903; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 713; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8829; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5283



July 8, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 21, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5539.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS 016781, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the orders under review are reversed. Parties are to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city and its manager, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), granting respondent city employee a writ of mandamus to set aside appellants' directive to remove respondent from his position as the chief of police and ordering appellants to grant a new administrative appeal hearing in compliance with the Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3300 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was the former chief of police for appellant city. Respondent was terminated from his position by appellant city manager. Afterwards, he was afforded an administrative appeal hearing before a hearing officer named by appellant city manager. Respondent was the only witness, and the hearing officer recommended respondent's termination. Respondent obtained a preemptory writ of mandate from the superior court ordering appellants to vacate their directive terminating respondent and ordering them to afford him a hearing that complied with the Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act (Act). On appeal, the court reversed the judgment, holding that respondent was afforded with a name clearing hearing, which was all that the Act and due process required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that the Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act required that only that an opportunity for administrative appeal be provided and did not specify how the appeal process was to be implemented. Further, the name clearing hearing was all that due process required.

 

1000 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Buhl v. Hannigan


No. G012245


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


16 Cal. App. 4th 1612; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 740; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 696; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5062; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8501; 2 Am. Disabilities Cas. (BNA) 1385



June 30, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 675268, James J. Alfano, Sr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying appellants' request for a preliminary injunction is affirmed. Appellants shall bear the costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant motorcyclists challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), denying appellants a preliminary injunction against enforcement, by respondent officials, of the state's Mandatory Motorcycle Helmet Law, Cal. Veh. Code §§ 27802 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant motorcyclists sought review of a ruling denying their motion to preliminarily enjoin respondents, Commissioner of the California Highway Patrol, Director of the Department of Motor Vehicles, Sheriff of Orange County, and Santa Ana Police Chief, from enforcing the Mandatory Motorcycle Helmet Law, Cal. Veh. Code §§ 27802 et seq., during the pendency of a suit challenging that law's constitutionality. The court held that: the state had a legitimate interest in highway safety; there was no fundamental right to operate a motor vehicle, including a motorcycle; the law was legitimately enacted pursuant to state police power for the purpose of preventing injuries to motorcyclists and their passengers; the law was rationally related to that goal; the law was not impermissibly vague; and the law had not unlawfully discriminated against handicapped persons, or impermissibly infringed on appellants' rights to privacy, freedom of religion, or freedom of expression. Finding no likelihood of success on the merits of appellants' challenge to the law, the court affirmed the lower court's denial of an injunction.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed. Appellant motorcyclists' motion to preliminarily enjoin enforcement, by respondent officials, of the state's mandatory helmet law was properly denied, because appellants did not demonstrate a likelihood of success on the merits of a constitutional challenge to the law, which was a reasonable exercise of the state's police power to promote public health, safety, and welfare.
